Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim
תלמוד ירושלמי שקלים
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> On the first of Adar one proclaims about <i>sheqalim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The yearly Temple tax of half a <i>sheqel</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-16" href="/Exodus.30.11-16"><i>Ex.</i> 30:11–16</a>). The expenses of the Temple service had to be paid from the tax collected for the year starting on Nisan 1. Since the tax could be enforced in the Land of Israel, it was sufficient to remind people of their duty and set up collection points one month in advance.<br>In talmudic times, and already in the LXX, the profane <i>sheqel</i> was identified with the Greek and Roman <i>didrachma</i>, or 2 denar piece, approximately equal to the Achemaenean <i>sheqel</i> (<i>siglos</i>). However, it is clear from the talmudic sources as well as the coinage of the first war against the Romans that as sacrificial <i>sheqel</i> the old Canaanite and Israelite <i>sheqel</i> was used, approximately a Roman tetradrachma (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1, Note 122</a>.), and therefore the biblical half-<i>sheqel</i> was a full profane <i>sheqel</i>.</i> and about <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is the end of the rainy season, farmers are reminded to remove spontaneous <i>kilaim</i> growth from their fields, so they should not run the risk that their harvest be forbidden for all use.</i>, and on the fifteenth of that month one reads the Esther scroll in fortified places<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χάραξ, “palisades”. The expression is used to describe walled cities reputed to have been walled at Joshua’s time, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:1:1-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.1.1-12">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i>1:1</a>.</i>, and one repairs the roads,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rural roads which might have been damaged during the rainy season, to prepare them for the pilgrims going to Jerusalem in the following month.</i> and the streets<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Municipal roads.</i>, and water pools<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the term is not discussed in the Halakhah, it is difficult to know whether one refers to religious <i>miqwaot</i> or to water supply for the pilgrims. Both kinds of pools may contain dust deposited there by the rains of the winter months.</i>, and one looks after all public needs<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Described in the Halakhah.</i>, and marks the graves<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Graves not in a cemetery, which must be clearly marked so ritually pure pilgrims will not be contaminated accidentally and then prevented from completing their pilgrimage. The whitewash characterizing these graves may have been damaged during the rainy season.</i>, and also goes to inspect for <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">As described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “On the first of Adar one proclaims,” etc. Why on the first of Adar? So that Israel should bring their <i>sheqalim</i> in time and it should be disbursed from the new contributions to the treasury<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The technical term for the Temple tax.</i> on time on the First of Nisan. And Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, [the heave of the treasury]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from the Babli. From here to the end of the paragraph there is a parallel in <i>Roš Haššanah</i>1:1 (56d line 4, <b>ר</b>).</i> as at its start, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 40:17" href="/Exodus.40.17"><i>Ex.</i> 40:17</a>.</i>, <i>it was in the First month of the Second year, on the first of the month, that the Sanctuary was erected.</i> It was stated hereto, on the day the Sanctuary was erected, on the same day the contribution<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple tax required in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-16" href="/Exodus.30.11-16"><i>Ex.</i> 30:11–16</a>.</i> was collected. Rebbi Tabi, Rebbi Yoshia in the name of Cahana. It is said here <i>months</i>, and it is said there, <i>months</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">As shown later, the reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:14" href="/Numbers.28.14"><i>Num</i>. 28:14</a>: <i>this is the monthly elevation offering on its day of the New Moon, for the months of the year</i>. This is compared to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:2" href="/Exodus.12.2"><i>Ex.</i> 12:2</a>, <i>this month shall be for you the start of months; it is the first of the months of the year</i>. The latter verse designates the month of the spring equinox as start of the year in all matters of sacrificial ritual.</i>. Since <i>months</i> mentioned there are only counted from Nisan, so also <i>months</i> mentioned here are only counted from Nisan. Rebbi Jonah said, Rebbi Tabi left out the start and only quoted the end; this is not done since it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In the way the <i>baraita</i> was stated by R. Tabi, it is impossible to see what it refers to. One has to state the <i>baraita</i> in its entirety for it to make sense. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 7a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.7a">Babli <i>Roš Haššanah</i>7a</a>.</i>: <i>This is the elevation sacrifice for a month at its New Moon</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:14" href="/Numbers.28.14"><i>Num</i>. 28:14</a>.</i>. I could think that one should collect every month, the verse says <i>at its New Moon for the months</i>; at one New Moon one collects for all months of the year. I could think on any month of his choosing, it is said here <i>months</i>, and it is said there, <i>months</i>. Since <i>months</i> mentioned there are only counted from Nisan, so also <i>months</i> mentioned here are only counted from Nisan.
What means “one lets hear”? Rav Huna said, one proclaims<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Hebrew expression was no longer understood, it is substituted by the Semitic <i>hif`il</i> of the Greek verb κηρύσσω.</i>, as you are saying<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 24:9" href="/II_Chronicles.24.9"><i>2Chr.</i> 24:9</a>.</i>, <i>they made a proclamation in Jehudah and Jerusalem</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">An almost exact parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:4:15-5:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.4.15-5.9"><i>Megillah</i> 1:7</a>, 71a line 21, <b>מ</b>.</i> There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i>1:7</a>.</i>: “The only difference between a First Adar and a Second Adar<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In an intercalary year, where a thirteenth month is added preceding the next Nisan, this “Second Adar” is the month where dates scheduled for Adar should be observed.</i> is the reading of the Esther scroll and gifts to the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Both in observance of the Purim festivities.</i>”. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, also proclamations regarding <i>sheqalim</i> and <i>kilaim</i> are between them<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Sheqalim</i>1:1 in an intercalary year refers to the first day of Second Adar.</i>. Rebbi Ḥelbo and Rav Ḥuna, Rav in the name of the Great Rabbi Ḥiyya: Everybody may fulfill his obligation on the Fourteenth, which is the time of its reading<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This has nothing to do with the topic under discussion. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:1:1-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.1.1-12">Mishnah Megillah 1:1</a> gives different dates for the reading of the <i>megillah</i> for different places. It is stated that the dates other than the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Adar are not absolute; the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> is valid at all places.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement of R. Simon.</i> is correct. Did they not say,. one proclaims about <i>sheqalim</i>, so that Israel should bring their <i>sheqalim</i> in time? If you would say on the First Adar, there still would be sixty days {left} in the year. Did they not say, also one goes to inspect for <i>kilaim</i>, not that the plants should be recognizable? If you would say in the First Adar, they still would be small.
Rebbi Ḥisqiah asked: But then the people in Babylonia proclaim about <i>sheqalim</i> on the start of the month<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In the version of the ms., Ḥizqiah points out that the Mishnah applied to countries outside the Holy Land leads to nonsensical results. In the version printed in the Babli, his is a declarative sentence, that in Babylonia one proclaims at the start of winter (about November), so the money may be delivered to Jerusalem in time.</i>. Was it not so that Israel should bring their <i>sheqalim</i> in time and it should be disbursed from the new contributions to the treasury on time on the First of Nisan? Rebbi Ulla asked before Rebbi Mana: Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 3:1:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.3.1.1-4">Mishnah 3:1</a>.</i>, “three times a year one disburses from the treasury, half a month before<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the Babli and the text of B, one reads פרס as (Latin) <i>pars</i>, “one half”, meaning half a month before the holiday indicated (Halakhah 3:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>).</i> Passover, before Pentecost, before Tabernacles.” He told him, we may say, before Passover, from those near; before Pentecost, from those farther away, before Tabernacles, from those still farther away. He answered him, it was collected all at one time. And why did they say, “three times a year”? To make a procession<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πομπή, Latin <i>pompa</i>.</i> of the occasion.
Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi in the name of Rebbi: May one read this and not be ashamed<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In B: afraid. In the biblical narrative, all good actions are ascribed to individuals, the bad to the entire people.</i>? In a good sense, <i>every one of goodwill</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 35:5" href="/Exodus.35.5"><i>Ex.</i> 35:5</a>.</i>; in a bad sense, <i>all the people took off the gold rings in their ears</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 32:3" href="/Exodus.32.3"><i>Ex.</i> 32:3</a>.</i>. In a good sense, <i>Moses led the people out</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 19:1" href="/Exodus.19.1"><i>Ex.</i> 19:1</a>.</i>; in a bad sense, <i>all of you ganged up against me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 1:22" href="/Deuteronomy.1.22"><i>Deut</i>. 1:22</a>.</i>. In a good sense, <i>then Moses and the Children of Israel sang</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:1" href="/Exodus.15.1"><i>Ex.</i> 15:1</a>.</i>; in a bad sense, <i>the entire congregation started wailing</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 14:1" href="/Numbers.14.1"><i>Num</i>. 14:1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, <i>indeed they got up early to destroy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zephaniah 3:7" href="/Zephaniah.3.7"><i>Zeph</i>. 3:7</a>.</i>. Any destructive action they made early in the morning. Rebbi Abba bar Aḥa said, one cannot understand the character of this people; they are asked for the {golden} calf and are giving, for the Sanctuary and are giving. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina stated this <i>baraita</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">To answer R. Ḥiyya bar Abba’s question.</i> <i>You shall make a cover of pure gold</i><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:17" href="/Exodus.25.17"><i>Ex.</i> 25:17</a>.</i>, may the gold of the cover come and atone for the gold of the calf.
Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman. Three contributions are mentioned in the paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 25:2-3" href="/Exodus.25.2-3"><i>Ex.</i> 25:2–3</a>, as explained in the text.</i>, the contribution for the bases<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">As noted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 38:27-28" href="/Exodus.38.27-28"><i>Ex.</i> 38:27–28</a>, the silver contributed by half-<i>sheqalim</i> contributed by a head tax according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-16" href="/Exodus.30.11-16"><i>Ex.</i> 30:11–16</a> was used to cast the bases, hooks, and covers, for the poles for the posts securing the sanctuary and its gobelins.</i>, the contribution for <i>sheqalim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since the receipt of the first tax was used for construction purposes, there must have been a second tax (in the amount of the first but collected only in the following years, not the year of construction) to provide for the communal sacrifices.</i>, and the contribution for the Sanctuary<sup class="footnote-marker">432</sup>. <i>Speak to the Children of Israel, they shall take for me a contribution</i>, that is the contribution for the bases. <i>From any man of goodwill take my contribution</i>, this refers to the contribution for <i>sheqalim</i>. <i>This is the contribution which you shall take from them</i>, that is the contribution for the Sanctuary. The contribution for the Sanctuary is for the Sanctuary, they may use it in any way they want. The contribution for <i>sheqalim</i> is for sacrifices, they may use it in any way they want, so that everybody’s part of it be the same. The contribution for the bases [is for the bases]<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Addition by the corrector following B but probably incorrect; the fact that it is equal for everybody distinguishes it from the contributions to the Sanctuary.</i>, <i>the rich may not increase and the poor may not decrease</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:15" href="/Exodus.30.15"><i>Ex.</i> 30:15</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abun said, also in this paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-16" href="/Exodus.30.11-16"><i>Ex.</i> 30:11–16</a>. The expression תְּרוּמָה לַיי or תְּרומַת יי appears in vv.. 13,14,15 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 29b" href="/Megillah.29b">Babli <i>Megillah</i>29b</a>.</i> are three contributions mentioned.
“On the fifteenth of this month one reads the Esther scroll in fortified places.” But did not Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rav Ḥuna, Rav in the name of the Elder Rabbi Ḥiyya, say<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup>: Everybody may fulfill his obligation on the Fourteenth, which is the time of its reading? It only comes to teach you that (the obligations apply to the Second Adar) [all obligations which apply to the Second Adar apply to the First Adar.]<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the scribe’s, the one in brackets the corrector’s adapted to the text of B. The scribe’s text is clear; since only one 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> and one 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> are mentioned, the reading of the scroll happens only once and this is the Second Adar of an intercalary year. In this version, in such a year Purim is not noticed at all in the First Adar. The corrector’s text and B take the opposite opinion, difficult to understand at this moment.</i> Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Aḥa were sitting together. Rebbi Yose said to Rebbi Aḥa, it only is reasonable for the past, not for the future<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The rule of R. Ḥiyya may only be applied to unforeseen circumstances. If people read on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> when according to the rules they should have read on the 15th, one does not tell them to read a second time. But if people from such a place come to ask before the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> whether they may read on the 14t<sup class="footnote-marker">h</sup>, one tells them no, they have to read on the 15<sup>th</sup>.</i>; as it was stated, at a place where one is used to read it both days<sup>49</sup><i class="footnote">In a city where there is a doubt whether one should read on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> or the 15th.</i> one reads it both days. He said to him, I also am of this opinion. Rebbi Mana said, this is correct. If one read it on the Fourteenth and then wants to read it<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In a city where there is a doubt whether one should read on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> or the 15th.</i> on the Fifteenth, would one not listen to him? If you are saying otherwise, you would eliminate the time of the fortified places with your hands<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Which is practice to be followed, being stated in two Mishnaiot.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel (original) text for this and the following paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:4:15-5:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.4.15-5.9"><i>Megillah</i>1:7</a>, 71a line 14 (<b>מ</b>).</i> It was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, Obligations which apply to the Second Adar do not apply to the First Adar except for eulogy and for fasting which are equal for both<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta <i>Megillah</i>1:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 6b" href="/Megillah.6b">Babli <i>Megillah</i> 6b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rav, Rebbi Simeon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Rebbi Ḥuna, the rabbi of Sepphoris said, Rebbi Ḥanina made it a custom in Sepphoris following Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. He only said custom, therefore not as practice<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">It is obligatory only in places which adopted the ruling.</i>.
But in matters of documents one writes the First Adar, the Second Adar. only that for the Second Adar one writes תִינייָן. Rebbi Jehudah says, the Second Adar one writes, and it is enough<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is thoroughly garbled, and the text of B is worse. The intelligible text is the original in <i>Megillah</i>(and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta <i>Megillah</i>1:6</a>). In the anonymous opinion (ascribed in B to R. Meïr), for both months one writes Adar, but the second one is qualified by “second” (in Aramaic, the language of contracts in both Talmudim.) R. Jehudah agrees that “Adar” alone means the First Adar in an intercalary year, but the Second may be indicated by a single letter ת֗, first letter of the Aramaic word “second”.</i>.
“One repairs the roads,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Rural roads which might have been damaged during the rainy season, to prepare them for the pilgrims going to Jerusalem in the following month.</i> and the streets<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Municipal roads.</i>, and water pools<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since the term is not discussed in the Halakhah, it is difficult to know whether one refers to religious <i>miqwaot</i> or to water supply for the pilgrims. Both kinds of pools may contain dust deposited there by the rains of the winter months.</i>, and one looks after all public needs<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Described in the Halakhah.</i>.” “<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Mo`ed Qaṭan</i>2:11. From here to the end of the Halakhah the text is from <i>Mo`ed Qaṭan</i>1:1 (80b 66), with no or little relevance here. The <i>Mo`ed Qaṭan</i> text of the ms. is indicated by <b>ק</b>; in addition there exist an Ashkenazic text of the Yerushalmi (A) edited by J. Sussman in <i>Kobez al Yad</i>12 (1994), pp. 62–63.<br>Most of the activities permitted in the <i>baraita</i> are for the benefit of individuals, not the public, most clearly delivering a new pair of shoes on the semi-holiday, mentioned last in the <i>baraita</i>. The only reason for its inclusion here is the identity of the expression “public needs”, applied both to public works in preparation for the holiday and permitted activities during the intermediate days of an extended holiday.</i> The following are the public needs: One judges civil suits, and capital crimes, and cases of flogging, and one redeems valuations<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Fixed amounts dedicated to the Temple, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:2-6" href="/Leviticus.27.2-6"><i>Lev.</i> 27:2–6</a>.</i>, and bans<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Dedications reserved for the Cohanim, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:14" href="/Numbers.18.14"><i>Num</i>. 18:14</a>.</i>, and dedications<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Dedications for the upkeep of the Temple, other than currency.</i>, one lets the suspected adulteress drink<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:11-31" href="/Numbers.5.11-31"><i>Num</i>. 5:11–31</a>.</i>, and burns the Cow<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>.</i>, and one breaks the neck of the calf whose neck was to be broken<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">For an unsolved murder case, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1–9</a>.</i>, and one pierces the ear of a Hebrew slave<sup>62</sup>, and one purifies the sufferer from skin disease<sup>63</sup>, and removes the shoe from the block but one may not return it.”
“One marks the graves.” Were they not already marked in Adar? Explain it if there was a flood caused by rain which rinsed it off<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This also is copied from <i>Mo`ed Qaṭan</i>. The question is why one should hire people to mark graves (or in the next paragraph to check for <i>kilaim</i>) on the semi-holidays approximately on the spring and fall equinoxes (Mishnah <i>Mo`ed Qaṭan</i>1:2) when they already had been checked in late winter. The reason that one tries to have all public works done on semi-holidays is that at that time most journeymen are without work and ready to work for lower wages. One provides work for the poor and saves taxpayer’s money at the same time. Cf. Babli <i>Mo`ed Qaṭan</i>6a.</i>.
“And also one goes to inspect for <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Graves not in a cemetery, which must be clearly marked so ritually pure pilgrims will not be contaminated accidentally and then prevented from completing their pilgrimage. The whitewash characterizing these graves may have been damaged during the rainy season.</i>.” Did they not already go in Adar? Explain it if the year was late and the plants not recognizable.
<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">This text in addition is in <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i>5:1, :Notes 17–25, <b>ש</b>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:1:5-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.1.5-8"><i>Sotah</i>9:1 (Notes 29–31</a>, <b>ס</b>). Babli <i>Mo`ed Qatan</i>6a. The biblical roots for the duty of the authorities to mark the places of graves with taxpayers’ money.</i> From where about marks? Rebbi Berekhiah, Rebbi Jacob the son of the daughter of Jacob, in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran. Rebbi Yose said it, Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Uziel the son of Rebbi Onias from Hauran in the name of Rebbi Onias from Hauran: <i>impure, impure, he shall call out</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:46" href="/Leviticus.13.46"><i>Lev.</i> 13:46</a>. An inappropriate reference since the verse refers to the impurity of the sufferers from skin disease, not of corpses.</i>; the impurity itself has to call out and say to you: go away! Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: <i>The emissaries shall crisscross the land; if one sees a bone of a human he builds a sign near it</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 39:15" href="/Ezekiel.39.15"><i>Ez</i>. 39:15</a>.</i> {<i>A bone</i>}<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Quoted from the parallels; in parallel to the following statements the source in the verse should be stated, except for the version of B which quotes the verse only up to <i>a bone</i>.</i>, from here that one makes signs for bones. <i>A human</i>, from here that one makes signs for spine and skull. <i>He builds</i>, from here that one makes signs on fixed stones. If you say on loose ones, it would move and make other places impure. <i>Near it</i>, on a place of purity. <i>A sign</i>, from here the marks.
<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Šeqalim</i>1:5</a>.</i> If one found a single marked stone, even though one should not keep it so, if somebody forms a tent over it he is impure; I say a marked corpse<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This expression has no explanation; B simply has “a corpse”, probably since the printer did not understand the word to be inserted. . Most parallels have קמצוץ “compressed”, a corpse buried with its head between the legs, so it fitted under the stone. This is not a Jewish burial custom. Since the majority of sources reads “impure”, they imply that Gentile corpses cause “tent impurity” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 61a" href="/Yevamot.61a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i>61a</a>); only the texts in <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> and the Tosephta follow R. Simeon who restricts tent (and any kind of biblical) impurity to Jewish bodies.</i> was under it. If there were two, he who forms a tent over any one of them is pure; between them he is impure. If between them was a ploughed strip they are single stones, between them the area is pure and around them<sup>62</sup> impure.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta <i>Šeqalim</i>1:5</a>.</i>: One does not mark flesh, for perhaps it will decompose<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Then it is no longer impure.</i>. Rebbi Justus bar Shunem asked before Rebbi Mana: Will that not cause pure food to be retroactively made impure<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the carrier of pure food learns that he has crossed a place where human flesh (without enough bone to cause tent impurity) was buried.</i>? He said to him, it is better that these should become unusable for a limited time than that {the earth} become unusable forever.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah says, originally they were uprooting and throwing down<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The public employees checking the fields for <i>kilaim</i> tore out all <i>kilaim</i> growth and left it lying on the fields.</i>. When the number of transgressors rose<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The farmers did not check for <i>kilaim</i> themselves since the public employees not only weeded the fields for them but also provided them with animal fodder.</i>, they were uprooting and throwing on the roadways<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Then the uprooted growth was no longer animal feed but at least they got the fields weeded on public expense.</i>; they instituted that they were declaring the entire field as ownerless<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Not the real estate but the crop growing on it.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Jehudah said.” It was stated: Rebbi Jehudah said, originally they were uprooting and throwing down before them; they were twice happy, first that they weeded their fields and second that they could use (the robbery) [the <i>kilaim</i> ]<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s [text] clearly is the correct one, supported by B; the scribe’s (text) possibly was caused by a lapse of attention.</i>. When the number of transgressors rose, they were throwing it on the roadways, and still they were happy that they weeded their fields. They instituted that they were declaring the entire field as ownerless.
From where that a declaration of forfeiture by a court makes it ownerless? <sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The following text is copied from <i>Peah</i>5:1 (<b>פ</b>). From the middle of the text there are two Genizah sources edited by Ginzberg without noting the readings differing from the <i>editio princeps</i>(<i>Yerushalmi Fragments from the Genizah</i>, New York 1909), one noted G (pp. 120,122,129,131), the other <b>ג</b> (pp. 121, 123–128,130,132–139).</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 10:8" href="/Ezra.10.8"><i>Ezra</i>10:8</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 89b" href="/Yevamot.89b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i>89b</a>.</i>: <i>Anybody who will not come within three days conforming to the decree of the rulers and the elders, all his property shall be devoted to destruction and he shall be separated from the community of the Diaspora</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">If the decree of the Court did not make the property abandoned, its destruction would have to be considered theft. Since Ezra was a teacher of the Law, his rulings have to be accepted.</i>. From where that it<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Produce declared ownerless by a decree of court. The intricacies of these rules are explained in <i>Peah</i>; they have no relevance here.</i> is free from tithes? Rebbi Jonathan, the son of Rebbi Isaac bar Aḥa understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.2.5">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i>2:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:23" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.23"><i>Sanhedrin</i> Yerushalmi 1:2 (Note 207</a>), Babli 12a; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.8.4"><i>Nedarim</i>6:13 Note 83</a>.</i>: “One intercalates<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Jewish year is both lunar and solar, but 12 lunar months are only approximately 254 days, in 19 years there have to be seven intercalary years of 13 months each. For details see the author’s <i>Seder Olam</i>(Jason Aronson, Northvale NJ, 1998).</i> years neither in the Sabbatical year, nor in the year following the Sabbatical; but if they did intercalate it is intercalated.” The one month he adds, is it not free from tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since the spontaneous growth of the Seventh Year may be taken by everybody, it is not <i>your harvest</i> and, hence, biblically free from heave and tithes even if taken by the owner of the land. If the Supreme Court declared the year intercalary against the rules, it is nevertheless a valid 13 months year and all produce is legally abandoned property.</i>? That refers to the Sabbatical year. What about the year after the Sabbatical? Rebbi Abun said, not to prolong the prohibition of new grain<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">New grain may be eaten only after the <i>Omer</i> sacrifice on the 16th of Nisan, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:14" href="/Leviticus.23.14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:14</a>. The intercalation of a month, which always falls in Adar, unnecessarily postpones the harvest of new grain.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is an aside, taken from <i>Ševi`it</i>6:4, also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:8:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.8.2-15"><i>Nedarim</i>6:13</a>. It explains why our calendar today does not take the Sabbatical year into consideration when determining intercalary months in the 19 year cycle.</i> Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: That is only before Rebbi permitted the importation of vegetables from outside the Land. But after Rebbi permitted the importation of vegetables from outside the Land, the Sabbatical year is as any other year<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The soil outside the Land of Israel is unclean (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 7:17" href="/Amos.7.17"><i>Amos</i>7:17</a>). In former times Jews did not use vegetables from outside the Land since it might have particles of soil still clinging to it. But after the last remnants of the ashes of the Red Cow disappeared, these laws became inoperative and it was possible for everybody, even the most scrupulous, to eat imported vegetables. This has to be dated to the times of Rebbi. Cf. commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.1">Mishnah Berakhot 1:1</a>.</i>.
It was stated: One does not intercalate in the Sabbatical Year [nor in the year following the Sabbatical;]<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, unjustified since the words also are missing in <b>ג</b> and in <i>Peah</i>.</i> but only in other years of a Sabbatical period, but if they did intercalate it is intercalated. Rebbi Mana said, that refers to earlier times when years were in order, but now that years are not in order<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Probably this means that since the Roman (Byzantine) government collects taxes from farmers also in the Sabbatical, they are forced to grow produce also in the Sabbatical; there no longer is any reason to treat the Sabbatical differently.</i>, the Sabbatical year is like any other year. It was stated: The house of Rabban Gamliel intercalated immediately after the end of the Sabbatical year. Rebbi Avin said, from this<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">All the previous arguments which prove that the action of the court can free produce from the Biblical obligations of heave and tithes are not relevant since there is a biblical obligation to manipulate the calendar so that Passover should fall in the month of the Spring equinox. Hence, the obligation to intercalate is biblical rather than rabbinic.</i> you do not infer anything. <i>watch the spring month</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:1" href="/Deuteronomy.16.1"><i>Deut</i>. 16:1</a>.</i>. Watch it that it should come in its renewal.
What text implies this<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Which Mishnah text implies that property decreed ownerless by the court is legally abandoned property?</i>? “For a grain stack under which gleanings were not collected, all ears that touch the ground are for the poor.”<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Peah</i>5:1.</i> Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: This is from the House of Shammai. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">With the text in <i>Peah</i> one has to read R. Yasa, contemporary of R. Immi, not R. Yose who has to be dated 2 generations later.</i> said to him: We understand that according to everybody, this is a fine<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.5.1.2"><i>Peah</i>5:1, Note 4</a>. There is no biblical reason why the ears touching the ground should be given to the poor; the farmer is fined since he put his sheaf on a place where the poor could not yet have taken the gleanings. The disagreement between the Houses of Shammai and Hillel is explained in <b>ג</b>: The House of Shammai hold that things abandoned exclusively for the use of the poor are legally abandoned whereas the House of Hillel hold that only things unconditionally abandoned are legally abandoned and therefore exempt from heave and tithes (Mishnah <i>Peah</i>6:1).</i>. Following the House of Hillel, the poor give tithes and eat.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> On the Fifteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Of Adar, in an intercalary year the Second Adar.</i>, money changers<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">To exchange coins into silver half-<i>sheqels</i>, half a Roman tetradrachma, which are collected for the Temple.</i> were sitting in the country side; on the Twenty Fifth they were sitting in the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody is permitted to sit in the sacred domain (except possibly kings of the Davidic dynasty), these money changers had to sit on the Temple Mount, outside the sacred domain.</i>. From the date they were sitting in the Temple one started to take pledges<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">To foreclose on people who did not pay their Temple tax.</i>. From whom does one take pledges? From Levites, Israel, proselytes, and freed slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">All these are adult male Jews subject to all commandments.</i>, but not from women, slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Who are obligated only in cases women are obligated. Since women do not pay, the <i>sheqel</i> being a positive commandment due at a fixed time, slaves cannot be obligated.</i>, and minors. Any minor for whom his father started to give the <i>sheqel</i> does not stop any more. However one does not take pledges from Cohanim because of communal peace<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>.<br>Rebbi Jehudah said: “Ben Kukhri testified at Jabneh that any Cohen who pays the <i>sheqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Voluntarily, even if they cannot be forced to pay.</i> does not sin. Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai told him, on the contrary, any Cohen who does not pay the <i>sheqel</i> does sin<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Since he reads the commandment <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-16" href="/Exodus.30.11-16"><i>Ex.</i> 30:11–16</a> as not stating any exemption for Cohanim.</i>, only the Cohanim explain the following verse for their benefit: <i>Any flour offering of a Cohen shall be totalled, it may not be eatenl</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:16" href="/Leviticus.6.16"><i>Lev.</i> 6:16</a>.</i>. If the <i>`omer</i>, the two breads, and the shew bread are ours, how may they be eaten?<i>l</i><sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">If they would pay the <i>sheqel</i>, they would be part owners of these offerings, but the consumption of all these offerings is explicitly commanded.</i>”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “On the Fifteenth,” etc. But one requests<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah states that one does not take pledges from women, slaves, and minors. It should have stated that these are not obligated to pay, then it would be obvious that nothing can be collected. This argument is valid for women and slaves, but since the Mishnah indicates that fathers are invited to pay the <i>sheqel</i> for their minor sons, an informal request may be made earlier, a formal one only if the son reaches the age of adulthood for religious obligations, the onset of puberty indicated by the growth of at least two pubic hairs.</i>? This is if he grew two pubic hairs, but it does not apply if he did not grow two pubic hairs. In matters of pledges, one never takes pledges if he did not grow two pubic hairs.
So is the Mishnah: One does not take pledges from Cohanim because one honors their status<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Even though they should pay as explained in the next Mishnah. The original text implies a criticism of the Cohanim; one does not enforce the payment because they are quarrelsome.</i>.
“Rebbi Jehudah said:, testified” etc. Rebbi Berekhiah said, the reason of Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai is<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:12" href="/Exodus.30.12"><i>Ex.</i> 30:12</a>.</i>, <i>this they shall give</i>, twelve tribes shall give. Rebbi Tabi in the name of Rav Hamnuna: So answer the Sages to Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Who reports the opinion of Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai approvingly.</i>. A private purification sacrifice is let to die; a public purification sacrifice is not left to die<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Once a sacrifice has been designated as a purification sacrifice it can neither be redeemed nor used for any other sacrifice. If such an animal was lost, another animal was used, and then the original was found again (or a few similar situations), nothing can be done with it, it must be left to die.</i>. A private flour offering<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Of a Cohen, as noted in the Mishnah.</i> is brought totally, but no public flour offering is brought totally. This is difficult, how can one argue with a person about something with which he does not agree<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><b>ג</b> reads: “by an argument that can be objected to.”</i>? “For no public purification sacrifice is left to die; Rebbi Jehudah says, it shall be left to die.<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 6:1:1-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.6.1.1-13">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i>6:2</a>; see there <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.1.6">Halakhah 1, Note 33</a>.</i>” And he objects to them, are these not private flour offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of Cohanim to which Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai objects implies that the half <i>sheqel</i> is not given as a tax but to acquire a minute part of the public sacrifices, which imply that there are no public offerings at all, only those of a private partnership.</i>? They answer him, from the moment when it is delivered to the public, it is treated as a public offering<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is impossible since biblical rules for private and public offerings differ in some respects.</i>. It is written, <i>everybody being counted</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:13" href="/Exodus.30.13"><i>Ex.</i> 30:13</a>.</i>, Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Neḥemiah. One said, everyone who crossed the Sea shall give, the other one said, everyone being counted<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Numbers</i> where it is made explicit that the tribe of Levi was not counted with the remainder of the tribes. Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai cannot explain this verse, unless he explains that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-16" href="/Exodus.30.11-16"><i>Ex.</i> 30:11–16</a> is not the basis of the duty to pay the <i>sheqel</i>, but Ben Kovri (Kokhri, Bukhri, Bikhri) cannot explain the inclusion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i> shall give. He who said, everyone who crossed the Sea shall give, supports Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai; he who said, everyone being counted shall give, supports Ben Kovri.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Even though they said that one does not take pledges from women, slaves, and minors, but if they gave the <i>sheqel</i> one accepts it from them. One does not accept from the Non-Jew and the Samaritan<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This is a statement in dispute as explained in the Halakhah. The argument behind the statement is that since the Temple tax is used for public sacrifices, only people for whom the sacrifices are brought may contribute.</i>, and from them one does not accept nests of male sufferers from gonorrhea, nests of female sufferers from flux, nests of women having given birth, and purification and reparation sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">This is the list of sacrifices which a Jew may only bring if he is obligated for them. Since a Non-Jew never is obligated to follow any pentateuchal rules other than the Noahide commandments, he never is eligible for these offerings. If a sacrifice is voluntary for Jews, it also is so for Non-Jews.</i>. This is the principle: Anything which can be given as a vow or a gift one does accept from them; anything which\can be given neither as a vow nor a gift one does not accept from them. So it is spelled out by Ezra, <i>it is not upon you and us to build our God’s House</i><sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 4:3" href="/Ezra.4.3"><i>Ezra</i> 4:3</a>. This refers only to the <i>sheqel</i>.</i>.<br>The following are liable for agio<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>collybus, collubus</i>, Greek κόλλυβος “exchange of coins, rate of exchange”, here used for the money changer’s fee.</i>: Levites, Israelites, proselytes, and freedmen, but not priests, women, slaves, and minors<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are obligated for the <i>sheqel</i>, they have to give exactly one didrachma piece. If they have other coins, they have to pay the fee for exchange in correct coins. But contributions of priests, women, slaves, and minors, are voluntary and not bound by the exact amount or exact coins and, therefore, do not have to be exchanged.</i>. He who pays the <i>sheqel</i> for a slave, a woman, a priest, or a minor, is not liable. He who paid the <i>sheqel</i> for himself and another person is liable for one agio; Rebbi Meïr says, two agios<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">For the rabbis, a person paying for two may pay a tetradrachma, R. Meïr requires two didrachmas.</i>. He who gives a tetradrachma to receive back a <i>sheqel</i> is liable for two agios<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">If he pays with a tetradrachma he should get back 2 <i>denar</i> minus the agio, set in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.4.1">Mishnah 7</a> either as an obolos (1/6 denar) or a semi-obolos. If he pays the money-changer’s fee separately, he has to pay for changing the common 2 denar into the less common didrachma.</i>.<br>A person who pays the <i>sheqel</i> for a poor person. a neighbor, or a dweller in his town is not liable for the agio, unless it is as a loan, then he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">If he pays for himself and from his own money he also pays for others, all is one transaction and pays one transaction fee. But if he is reimbursed for his expense this is not an act of charity; therefore each payment is a separate transaction and incurs a separate fee.</i>. Brothers who are partners<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah the reading from a different Mishnah is: “brothers or partners”, but B quoting the Mishnah here reads “brothers as partners”. As long as the father’s estate is not distributed, the surviving brothers are still considered children of their father; if the estate pays for their <i>sheqel</i> it is one transaction and incurs one fee. Calves born to the estate are considered property of one person and the tenth calf automatically becomes a sacrifice. If they distributed the estate but then decided to continue jointly to pursue the agricultural affairs of their father, they form a new partnership and follow the rules of partnerships. They are independent persons, each of whom has to pay his own fee, but all calves born are property of joint owners, not a single person, therefore the tenth calf or lamb (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:32" href="/Leviticus.27.32"><i>Lev.</i> 27:32</a>) is not sanctified.</i> who are liable for the agio are not liable for animal tithe; if they are liable for animal tithe they are not liable for the agio. How much is the agio? A silver obolos<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">1/6 of a Roman denar.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr; but the Sages say, half an obolos.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Even though they said,” etc. Therefore one does not request? Here you are saying, one requests, but there<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah states that one does not take pledges from women, slaves, and minors. It should have stated that these are not obligated to pay, then it would be obvious that nothing can be collected. This argument is valid for women and slaves, but since the Mishnah indicates that fathers are invited to pay the <i>sheqel</i> for their minor sons, an informal request may be made earlier, a formal one only if the son reaches the age of adulthood for religious obligations, the onset of puberty indicated by the growth of at least two pubic hairs.</i> you are saying, one does not request. There if he grew two pubic hairs, and here if he did not grow two pubic hairs.
“One does not accept from the Non-Jew and the Samaritan.” Rebbi Abba said, explain it<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">On the face of it, the equation of Samaritans with Gentiles is unintelligible. The <i>baraita</i> shows that up to the time of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, Rebbi’s father, the Samaritans were simply Sadducee Jews, the only Sadducee sect to survive after the destruction of the Temple. The Mishnah here reproduces Rebbi’s opinion. The paragraph also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 7:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.7.1.8"><i>Berakhot</i>7:1 (Note 59</a>, <b>ב</b>).</i> following him who said, a Samaritan is like a Gentile, as they disagreed: A Samaritan is like a Gentile, the words of Rebbi; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, a Samaritan is like a Jew in all respects.
Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah is about Gentiles, therefore not about Samaritans. It was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Wayyiqra I (Ḥovah) Pereq</i>2(3).</i>: “<i>A human</i><sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:2" href="/Leviticus.1.2"><i>Lev.</i> 1:2</a>. This use of <i>human</i> follows R.. Simeon’s interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 34:30" href="/Ezekiel.34.30"><i>Ez</i>. 34:30</a>, where he reads אָדָ֣ם אַתֶּ֑ם as “you are noblemen” (Accadic <i>awēlum</i>), a title reserved for members of the Covenant. Since the Samaritans are descendants of proselytes, they are included in all obligations and privileges of the covenant and cannot be excluded from any of these.</i>, to include the proselytes. <i>From among you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Reading prefix <i>mem</i> as privative; excluding people who removed themselves from the Covenant. It seems that <b>ג</b> reads “removed from discipline”.</i>, to exclude the apostates.” The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Eleazar: “One does not accept nests of male sufferers from gonorrhea, nests of female sufferers from flux, nests of women having given birth.” Do there exist nests of sufferers from gonorrhea and flux among Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">These kinds of impurity do not apply to Gentiles who anyhow do not need them since they only are required to permit the healed person to enter the sanctuary or eat sancta, from which Gentiles are excluded.</i>? But the beginning is about Gentiles, the end about Samaritans<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The statement that one does not accept the <i>sheqel</i> from them (but also that one accepts their voluntary sacrifices.) The rest is Rebbi’s formulation to exclude Samaritans from Jewish worship.</i>. So it is, the beginning is about Gentiles, the end about Samaritans.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, at the start<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">At the start of building the Temple, the situation described in the verse from <i>Ezra</i>. While the verse refers to Samaritans, the discussion here is about Gentile offerings.</i> one accepts from them neither definite objects nor non-definite objects<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">After the building was finished and funds are needed for its continual upkeep.</i>, and at the end one accepts from them definite objects but not non-definite objects<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Torah clearly accepts Gentile sacrifices, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:25" href="/Leviticus.22.25"><i>Lev.</i> 22:25</a>, one also has to accept vessels or other objects inscribed with the Gentile donor’s name. But unspecified moneys for the continual upkeep of the Temple are reserved for and are a duty upon Jews.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, both at the beginning and at the end one accepts from them neither definite objects nor non-definite objects. A <i>baraita</i><sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta 1:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Section 7 2" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Section_7.2"><i>Sifra Emor Parashah</i>7(2</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 73b" href="/Menachot.73b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i>73b</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.1.2"><i>Nazir</i>9:1 Note 8</a>.</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “One does not accept from them voluntary gifts for Temple property for the upkeep of the Temple.” He explains it, both at the start and at the end, if it is for non-definite objects. A Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Arakhin 1:3" href="/Mishnah_Arakhin.1.3">Mishnah <i>Arakhin</i>1:3</a>, where R. Meïr and R. Jehudah disagree about the status of a Gentile with respect to the rules detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:2-8" href="/Leviticus.27.2-8"><i>Lev.</i> 27:2–8</a>.</i> disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Everybody agrees that they make vows and are objects of vows<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since a voluntary sacrifice must be vowed to the Temple before it can be offered, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:25" href="/Leviticus.22.25"><i>Lev.</i> 22:25</a> clearly implies that a Gentile’s vows are valid in a Jewish setting. It is stated that a Jew may make a vow whose object is a Gentile or which is conditioned on the actions of a Gentile.</i>. He explains it for elevation offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">These are the only sacrifices which a Gentile unquestionably is able to bring. It is difficult to see how he could bring a well-being offering which as a family sacrifice has to be consumed by the pure family members. The Gentile, being biblically unable to be impure, cannot biblically be pure either.</i>. One understands that he makes a vow to bring an elevation offering. Can he be the object of a vow for an elevation offering? No, if an Israel says, I undertake to bring an elevation offering, when a Gentile hears him and says, I am undertaking what he says<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">While the Gentile is not the passive object of a vow, his vow is subsidiary to the Jew’s.</i>. Does he not bring libations with it<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">As required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:1-15" href="/Numbers.15.1-15"><i>Num</i>. 15:1–15</a>.</i>? Is not the excess money given for libations used for vessels of Service? Then it turns out that he brought {money for} a definite object<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody brings his libations to the Temple; he pays for them in the Temple; they are brought from the Temple’s stores, and the net proceeds are used to buy gold and silver vessels for the Temple. These are objects that could be engraved with the donor’s name.</i>! Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun objected, did we not state that they may offer their value<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">While not mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:2-8" href="/Leviticus.27.2-8"><i>Lev.</i> 27:2–8</a>, in fact this is what money donations to the Temple are used for.</i>? Are offerings of one’s value not for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The person making the vow of his value is intent to give the money to the worship; what actually is done with the money is not of interest to him.</i>? It is as you are saying there, his intent was for Heaven; automatically it will be used for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, the excess money given for libations goes into a big pot where the individual contributions are no longer recognizable; no donor’s plate can be affixed to any vessel bought with such money.</i>; so here you are saying, his intent was for Heaven; automatically it will be used for vessels of Service. How does Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish treat this? He explains, <i>it is not upon you and us to build our God’s House</i>. Rebbi Ḥilkiah said, Rebbi Simon asked: Does this imply that one does not accept from them<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, the excess money given for libations goes into a big pot where the individual contributions are no longer recognizable; no donor’s plate can be affixed to any vessel bought with such money.</i> for an aqueduct, or the city walls, or its towers, because of <i>you have no part, nor rightful claim, nor memorial, in Jerusalem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 2:20" href="/Nehemiah.2.20"><i>Neh</i>. 2:20</a>.</i>.
“The following are liable for agio,” etc. The Mishnah is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr said, even though his <i>sheqel</i> is not from the Torah, his agio is from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to accept the reading of B and <b>ג</b>: “just as his <i>sheqel</i> is from the Torah” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-16" href="/Exodus.30.11-16"><i>Ex.</i> 30:11–16</a>). The argument of the concluding <i>aggadah</i> is that the obligation is not to give the value of a half-<i>sheqel</i> of silver (5.7 g), but the exact coin which at this moment is called “<i>sheqel</i>” having approximately the historical weight. Then naturally anybody who pays with other coins automatically is required to pay the statutory fee for exchange into the correct coin.</i>. Rebbi Meir is of the opinion that one who gives his <i>sheqel</i> as a piece is not liable for the agio<sup class="footnote-marker">150 151</sup>, Rebbi Meïr said, like a kind of coin made of fire did the Holy One, praise to Him, bring out from under the Throne of Glory and showed it to Moses. He said to him, <i>this they shall give</i><sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:13" href="/Exodus.30.13"><i>Ex.</i> 30:13</a>.</i>, like this they shall give.
“He who gives a tetradrachma to receive back a <i>sheqel</i> is liable for two agios.” Rebbi Eleazar said, it is Rebbi Meïr’s, as Rebbi Meïr said, one for the <i>sheqel</i> which he gives and one for the tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">With the other two sources, read: <i>sheqel</i>.</i> which he takes. Rav said, it is everybody’s opinion, one for the <i>sheqel</i> which he [gives, one for the <i>sheqel</i> which he]<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">Addition by the corrector following B; this has to be deleted since it makes the question about Rav unnecessary.</i> takes, and one for the words of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Rav insists that anybody who pays with any coin other than a didrachma first has to pay the regular fee for the money changer (into the money changer’s pocket) and then an additional agio (into the Temple’s treasury) for not presenting a didrachma; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta 1:8</a>, end. According to him, the κολλυβιστής “agio collector” in the Temple (<i>Matt</i>. 21:12) collected the agio for the Temple treasury and with good reason is not called τραπεζίτης “money changer”.</i>. In Rav’s opinion, are there three agios? There came Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac in the name of Rav: There are three agios, one for the <i>sheqel</i> which he gives, one for the <i>sheqel</i> which he takes, and one for the words of the Torah.
Brothers or partners who are liable for the agio and not liable for animal tithe,” if they distributed and then formed a partnership; “if they are liable for animal tithe and not liable for the agio,” if they never distributed.
“A person who pays the <i>sheqel</i> for a poor person,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said, only if they counted lambs against rams and rams against lambs, but if they counted lambs against lambs and rams against rams, it was his part from the first hour. Rebbi Joḥanan said, even if they counted lambs against lambs and rams against rams, they are like buyers<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eleazar accords them the state of partnership only it there was a genuine distribution of the estate before the new partnership was entered into; if the estate was left untouched and each son got a proportional share of everything, it remains an estate. R. Joḥanan holds that the formal establishment of a partnership is all that is needed. Here “property” means not only real estate but everything not livestock. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 56b" href="/Bekhorot.56b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i>56b</a>.</i>, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 9:3" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.9.3">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i>9:3</a>.</i>: “The buyer or recipient of a gift is not liable for animal tithe. Rebbi Ḥizkiah<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of <b>ג</b> “Ḥilkiah” has to be rejected since the latter was not a student of R. Jeremiah.</i> said that Rebbi Jeremiah asked: And why are we not saying that sometimes they are liable for both and sometimes they are not liable for either. How is this done? If they distributed the properties and afterwards distributed the animals they are liable for both<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">If the estate was distributed except for livestock and then a partnership formed, they are individually responsible for their <i>sheqels</i> with agio, while the undistributed livestock remains subject to animal tithe even under the new arrangement.</i>. If they distributed the animals and afterwards distributed the properties they are not liable for either.<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">If the livestock was distributed but not the real estate and the cash of the estate, they may continue to pay their <i>sheqels</i> together with one agio while the livestock is under the rules of partnership. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 56b" href="/Bekhorot.56b">Babli <i>Bekhorot</i>56b</a>.</i> Rebbi Mana said, this holds only if the animals were not a majority, but if the animals were a majority they form the main property<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The rule of the Mishnah applies even if the cash was never distributed explicitly.</i>. Rebbi Abun said that Rebbi Shammai asked: Because you made them like one person for animal tithe, you made them not liable for agios<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">If the heirs are adults the status of the estate should have no influence on the duty to pay the agio.</i>? He said to him, no. There is a difference because he is giving a complete tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">Since the estate (here supposed to be property of two brothers) pays for both of them, there is only one transaction and only one fee is due.</i>. Then even if they distributed and then formed a partnership, they should be liable for animal tithe and not liable for the agio<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">If the partnership would pay for them.</i>. But we have stated, “they are liable for the agio, not liable for animal tithe.” Rebbi Abba in the name of Abba bar Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli he is mentioned as Rabbah bar Rav Huna.</i> it is the same for two brothers inheriting from their father or two brothers-in-law inheriting from their father-in-law<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the absence of male offsprings the daughters inherit following the rules for males, all rules for brothers dividing the estate of their father apply to brothers-in-law acting as administrators of their wives’ estates.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta 1:8</a>, end.</i> “Where were the agios credited to? Rebbi Meïr says, to the <i>sheqalim</i>. Rebbi Eleazar says, for voluntary offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote"> “Gift,” the separate account kept at the Temple treasury from which sacrifices were bought to occupy the altar in case it otherwise would be idle.</i>. Rebbi Simeon from Shezur says, gold sheeting, cover for the Holiest of Holies<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">This special account in the Temple treasury is not mentioned in any other source.</i>. Ben Azzai says, the money changers were taking them as their fees; but some say, for travel expenses<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody except Ben Azzai holds that the money changers as tax collectors are salaried employees of the Temple. In the second version of Ben Azzai’s opinion he also agrees that the tax collectors receive a basic salary for the time they are working for the Temple; the income from the agio only covers their travel expenses.</i>.”

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One consolidates <i>sheqalim</i> to <i>darics</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Δαρεικός, a Persian gold coin; here the name stands for the Roman <i>aureus</i>, commonly worth 25 full weight silver <i>denarii</i>.</i> because of the difficulty of transport. Just as there were horns<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Boxes in the shape of horns wirh wide bottoms and narrow slits to deposit the coins.</i> in the Temple, so were in the countryside. If the people of a city sent their <i>sheqalim</i> and these were stolen or lost, if the sums were disbursed<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If the new <i>sheqalim</i> were taken from the treasury to be disbursed for the service of the new year, in the computation of the monies available in Nisan the collected but not received sums are taken into account for disbursing on the next two occasions (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.1.5">Chapter 1, Note 26</a>). Therefore the money was stolen from the Temple; the oath which is required either by biblical law or by rabbinic institution from the carrier to be sworn in presence of the owner or his representative, has to be sworn in presence of the Temple treasurers. If the theft or loss was detected earlier, the oath is due to the representatives of the taxpayers. The oaths are different for salaried or unpaid carriers.</i> they will have to swear to the Temple custodians<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The administrators of the Temple treasury.</i>, otherwise they will have to swear to the city dwellers who have to replace them by new <i>sheqalim</i>. If they were found or the thieves returned them<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If the <i>sheqalim</i> already were Temple property (as explained in the preceding sentence), they would have committed not only larceny but also sacrilege.</i>, both are <i>sheqalim</i> and cannot be credited for the coming year.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One consolidates <i>sheqalim</i> to <i>darics</i>,” etc. Should one exchange them for a pearl? Perhaps the pearl would lose in value and the Temple treasury would suffer damage. As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 8:7" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.8.7">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i>8:7</a>.</i>, “All<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Any <i>sancta</i> which may be redeemed at all may be redeemed for money’s worth except <i>sheqalim</i>.</i> can be redeemed by money or monies-worth except for <i>sheqalim</i>” [and one does not redeem by implements;]<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Addition by the corrector from the Babli, unnecessary as already noted by R. Eliahu of Wilna.</i> and Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, perhaps the implements would lose in value and the Temple treasury would suffer damage. Also here, perhaps the pearl would lose in value and the Temple treasury would suffer damage.
The Mishnah is about new <i>sheqalim</i>, but not about old <i>sheqalim</i>. And it was stated so: Old ones in the Temple, no old ones in the countryside<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Only current taxes are collected outside of Jerusalem; taxes due from prior years have to be delivered by the taxpayer, who has to bear the full risk of accidents before delivery, to the Temple in Jerusalem.</i>. The Mishnah is about an unpaid trustee, but not about a paid keeper<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševuot</i> 8:1. The unpaid trustee has to swear that he did not take the valuables for himself and that he was not grossly negligent. The paid keeper has to pay for what is lost or stolen; there is no reason he should swear in these circumstances.</i>. Rav Abba said, even if you say<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since the speaker is a Babylonian, the reading of <b>ג</b>, תימא, may be a true report of his saying and not a babylonized spelling of a Yerushalmi text.</i> about a paid keeper, if they were stolen, by an armed robber<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ληστής. The paid keeper does not have to pay for accidents which clearly were beyond his control. Babli <i>Bava Meṣi`a</i>58a.</i>, if the were lost, as when his ship sank at sea. Rebbi Justus ben Rebbi Simon said, it follows him who said, one disburses on the account of what was collected and what was to be collected<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 108a" href="/Ketubot.108a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>108a</a>, <i>Bava Meṣi`a</i>58a.</i>, but not following him who said one does not disburse on basis of what was collected<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">But not delivered to the Temple treasury.</i> nor what was secured by pledges.
“If the people of a city sent their <i>sheqalim</i> and these were stolen or lost.” <sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since <b>ג</b> and the Babli version M confirm the text of the original scribe, the corrector’s additions have to be considered as a conflation with the text of B.</i> Rebbi Eleazar said, this is Rebbi Simeon’s, since Rebbi Simeon said, <i>sancta</i> for whose alienation he is responsible are like his own property<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Shevuot 6:7" href="/Mishnah_Shevuot.6.7">Mishnah <i>Ševuot</i>6:7 (Note 97</a>). The majority opinion is that there can be no (biblical) oath for <i>sancta</i>; so it seems that for them the carriers of the <i>sheqalim</i> could not swear.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is everybody’s opinion, because of an instituted oath<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion the Mishnah <i>Ševuot</i> is irrelevant here. The oath is not biblical, and it is not a question whether the <i>sheqalim</i> are <i>sancta</i> or not; the oath is the general rabbinic oath imposed on all persons who cannot be forced to pay damages and on whom no biblical oath can be imposed.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion is understandable; “they have to swear to the city dwellers who have to replace them by new <i>sheqalim.</i>” Is that Rebbi Simeon’s? Even though the city dwellers agreed to pay, Temple property cannot be released without an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the city dwellers believe the trustees and do not require an oath that they are innocent of the loss, it is a rabbinic requirement that the Temple trustees insist on such an oath.</i>.
Somebody put aside his <i>sheqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Before it was delivered to the Temple authorities.</i> and it was lost. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he is responsible for its alienation<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">He has to pay another <i>sheqel</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 139a" href="/Chullin.139a">Babli Ḥulin 139a</a>.</i> until he delivers to the Temple trustee. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, Temple property is Heaven’s at any place where it may be<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i>1:6</a>: “A promise to Heaven is like delivery to an individual.”</i>. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “they have to swear to the city dwellers who have to replace them by new <i>sheqalim.</i>.” Still<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, supported by all parallel sources.</i> this is because of a regulation oath<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The oath is only because they lost other people’s property, and the city dwellers pay as a matter of public policy; it does not imply anything about personal liability to replace the lost money.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">B (not supported by M) reads: “Rebbi stated”.</i>: The first ones fall to the new <i>sheqalim</i>, the second ones to the old <i>sheqalim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of the last sentence of the Mishnah. The old <i>sheqalim</i> are the leftover from the previous year, given to the gift fund for supplementary sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:4:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.4.10">Chapter 1, Note 171</a>.)</i>. Which ones are first and which ones are second? Rebbi Phineas ben Rebbi Ḥananiah<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Abba Mari belongs to the last generation of Galilean Amoraim, he also has to be dated in this period. His exact patronymic cannot be determined.</i> and Rebbi Abba Mari. One said, those that the city dwellers sent first<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli texts B and M add: “therefore the last”; the <i>sheqalim</i> contributed first are the last to be delivered. This seems to be a gloss incorporated into the text.</i>, but the other one said, those which came in to the hands of the Temple trustees first.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one gives his <i>sheqel</i> to another person to deliver it for him but that one uses it for himself, if the sums were disbursed he committed <i>me`ilah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrilegious larceny.<br>As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.1.1">Note 3</a>, the money used for public sacrifices, disbursed three times a year (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 3:1:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.3.1.1-4">Mishnah 3:1</a>) is computed taking into account monies collected but not yet delivered. These therefore are Temple money wherever they be at the moment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 2:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.2.1.5">Note 22</a>).
