Therefore one has to be inclusive in the declaration.</i> and in the name of all of Israel. The third time in the name of Babylonia and the name of Media and in the name of all of Israel. It was stated: If he took from the first box, even if there was in the first box he shall take from the second<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This does not refer to the three times a year one disburses from the lodge but to the three boxes which are filled with coins, which have to be used in the correct order.</i>. If he took from the second box, even if there was in the second box he shall take from the third. If the third was used up, he returns to the second; if the second was used up he returns to the first. If all three are emptied, one collects new <i>sheqalim</i>; Rebbi Meïr says, he has regress on the remainder<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the excess coins had been declared remainder and property of the gift account, actually they become remainder only at the start of a new tax year. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shekalim (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Shekalim_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a> in the name of R. Simeon ben Eleazar, 2:1 as everybody’s opinion.</i>, since Rebbi Meïr says, one commits <i>me`ilah</i> with the remainder,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since the money remains potentially dedicated to be used for public sacrifices.</i> perhaps one needs them at the end.
<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph has been copied by the corrector from B. It is neither in the scribe’s text nor in <b>ג</b>; it is a Babylonian addition. It is a slight rewrite of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 1:3:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.1.3.11"><i>Šabbat</i>1, Notes 274–286</a>, based on the Babylonian version of the last Mishnah in <i>Soṭah</i>.</i>[“And so did Rebbi Phineas ben Yair say, promptitude brings to cleanliness, cleanliness brings to purity, purity brings to holiness, holiness brings to meekness, meekness brings to fear of sin, fear of sin brings to piety, piety brings to the Holy Spirit, the Holy Spirit to the Resurrection of the Dead, the Resurrection of the Dead brings to Elijah, may his remembrance be a blessing.” “Promptitude brings to cleanliness,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:20" href="/Leviticus.16.20"><i>Lev.</i> 16:20</a>.</i>, <i>he finishes, and he atones</i>. “Cleanliness brings to purity,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:8" href="/Leviticus.12.8"><i>Lev.</i> 12:8</a>.</i>, <i>the Cohen shall atone for her, then she will be pure</i>. “Purity brings to holiness,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:19" href="/Leviticus.16.19"><i>Lev.</i> 16:19</a>.</i>, <i>he shall purify it and sanctify it</i>. “Holiness brings to meekness,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 57:15" href="/Isaiah.57.15"><i>Is.</i> 57:15</a>.</i>, <i>for so says the High and Elevated One, Who thrones eternally, His name is Holy, in sublimity … and the oppressed and of meek spirit.</i>“Meekness brings to fear of sin,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 22:4" href="/Proverbs.22.4"><i>Prov.</i>22:4</a>.</i>, <i>the consequence of meekness is fear of the Eternal</i>. “Fear of sin brings to piety,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 103:17" href="/Psalms.103.17"><i>Ps.</i> 103:17</a>.</i>, <i>the Eternal’s piety is eternally on those who fear Him</i>. “Piety brings to the Holy Spirit,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 89:20" href="/Psalms.89.20"><i>Ps.</i> 89:20</a>.</i>, <i>then You spoke in a vision to Your pious ones</i>. “The Holy Spirit brings to the Resurrection of the Dead,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 37:14" href="/Ezekiel.37.14"><i>Ez</i>. 37:14</a>.</i>, <i>I shall give My Spirit into you and you will live</i>. “The Resurrection of the Dead brings to Elijah, may his remembrance be a blessing,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Misquote of <i>Pr</i>. 2:5.</i>, <i>then you will understand the fear of the Eternal, and the knowledge of God you will find</i>. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr: Anybody permanently in the Land of Israel who speaks the holy language, eats its produce in purity, and recites the <i>Shema`</i> mornings and evenings is assured of his place in the World to Come.]

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> For what was the disbursement used? One buys from it daily and <i>musaf</i> sacrifices and their libations, the <i>`omer</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The offering of barley grain on the 16th of Nisan to permit consumption of grain from the new harvest; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:9-14" href="/Leviticus.23.9-14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:9–14</a>.</i>, and the Two Breads<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The two leavened breads on Pentecost, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:17" href="/Leviticus.23.17"><i>Lev.</i> 23:17</a>.</i>, and the shew-bread<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Seven breads every week, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:5-9" href="/Leviticus.24.5-9"><i>Lev.</i> 24:5–9</a>.</i>, and all public offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Including incense and public reparation offerings (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13-21" href="/Leviticus.4.13-21"><i>Lev.</i> 4:13–21</a>.)</i>. The watchmen for aftergrowth in the sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>`omer</i> and the Two Breads have to be produce of the Land, and no sowing is permitted in a Sabbatical, the grains have to be harvested from spontaneous aftergrowth. Fields from which such a yield is expected have to be watched lest the plants be eaten by animals or the grains be taken by humans. In this case the watch is an essential precondition for the possibility of the offering and therefore the expenses have to be borne by the Temple.</i> take their wages from the disbursement from the lodge. Rebbi Yose says, he also may volunteer as unpaid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the unpaid trustee acquires rights to what he is watching, R. Yose holds that private property may be donated to the Temple as public offering.</i>. They told him, would you not also say that they only come from the public<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages deny that private property may be donated to the Temple as public offering. “They” are public offerings. Therefore the argument cannot be intended to convince R. Yose; rather it is a statement clarifying that they insist that only fully paid watchmen are acceptable.</i>?
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup>“For what was the disbursement used,” etc. For what reason were the times of wood by the priests and the people to be counted<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.4.2">Halakhah <i>Ta`aniot</i>4:6</a> (<b>ת</b>), also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.2.2"><i>Megillah</i>1:2</a> (70c l. 1 ff.; <b>מ</b>). In fact, <b>ג</b> simply notes: “one repeats from <i>Ta`aniot</i> up to ‘and were fasting but not completing.’ ”</i>? Only that at the time when Israel returned from the Diaspora and did not find wood in the chamber, those came forward and volunteered wood from their own, donated it to the public, and used it to offer public sacrifices. The prophets among them stipulated that even if the chamber was full of wood and those came and offered and volunteered wood from their own, that the sacrifice should only be brought first from theirs<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Ta`anit</i>28a.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, this is Rebbi Yose’s, since Rebbi Yose said, also he may volunteer as unpaid trustee. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Yose the fifth generation Amora. The reading of B (and M), R. Assi (= Yasa), is impossible since R. Ila was a student, not a teacher, of R. Yasa.</i> in the name of Rebbi Ila, it is the opinion of everybody. Where do they disagree? About the body of the offering. But for enablers of the offering everybody agrees that a private offering can be turned into public offering<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">While holding that the firewood is ancillary, not intrinsic, to the sacrifice, they still would have to require that the two daily logs required before any sacrifices are brought to the altar (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:5" href="/Leviticus.6.5"><i>Lev.</i> 6:5</a>)to be paid for from the public purse.</i>. It was stated, a woman who made a coat for her son has to surrender it to the public<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the prescribed garments of a priest are part of the establishment of the Tabernacle (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:40-43" href="/Exodus.28.40-43"><i>Ex.</i> 28:40–43</a>), they must be public property. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 35b" href="/Yoma.35b">Babli <i>Yoma</i>35b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, this is Rebbi Yose’s, since Rebbi Yose said, also he may volunteer as unpaid trustee. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ila, it is the opinion of everybody. Where do they disagree? About the body of the offering. But for enablers of the offering everybody agrees that a private offering can be turned into public offering.. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Amora, speaking in the name of R. Ila.</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ta`aniot</i>3:6.</i>“Those days are observed at the time of sacrifices and not at the time of sacrifices; Rebbi Yose says, they are observed only at the time of sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The days enumerated on which certain families celebrated their offerings of firewood according to the Tanna R. Yose are treated as days of sacrificing. Therefore at least for him, firewood is part of the sacrifice, not ancillary, and may be given from private property as public sacrifice.</i>” Also from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ta`aniot</i>3:6.</i>: “Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Ṣadoq said, we were of the descendants of Senaah ben Benjamin. When the Ninth of Av fell on a Sabbath, we postponed it to the end of the Sabbath and were fasting but not completing.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Their holiday was on the 10th of Av. Since the 9th of Av is the day of remembering the destruction of the Temple, his story must be dated after the destruction, when there were no longer any sacrifices. If the family holiday is so important that one pushes aside the fasting for the 9th of Av, it must be that even for the majority the offering of firewood was the equivalent of a sacrifice, not an ancillary act. Therefore R. Aḥa is justified.</i>”
“The <i>`omer</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The offering of barley grain on the 16th of Nisan to permit consumption of grain from the new harvest; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:9-14" href="/Leviticus.23.9-14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:9–14</a>.</i>, and the Two Breads<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The two leavened breads on Pentecost, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:17" href="/Leviticus.23.17"><i>Lev.</i> 23:17</a>.</i>., and the shew-bread.” The Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The part of the Mishnah which states that the watchmen over grain for the <i>`omer</i> are paid from public funds. If it were possible to import the grain during a sabbatical year, the expense would be unnecessary and therefore forbidden.</i> is Rebbi Ismael’s, since Rebbi Ismael said, the <i>`omer</i> is not brought from Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Syria in matters of religious law is the area which was part of David’s empire but not permanently settled by one of the Twelve Tribes. The rules of the Land do not apply biblically but the land is not ritually impure. Therefore grain for use in the Temple could be bought from there.</i>. There, we have stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 8:1" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.8.1">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i>8:1</a>. The sacrifices referred to are offerings of flour and wine.</i> “All private and public sacrifices come from the Land and from outside the Land, from new or old [grain], except for `<i>omer</i> and the Two Breads, which only come from new grain<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is not obvious since the <i>`omer</i> permits the profane use of new grain also from outside the Land and the Two Breads permit the use of new wheat in the Tabernacle which according to the Mishnah includes wheat imported from Syria. While the Mishnah states that grain is acceptable from outside the Land, this refers to grain from outside the Land on both sides of the Jordan and Syria only if it remained impervious to the impurity of Gentile lands (i. e., if it was guarded from any contact with water or fluids whose status is like water in this respect.)</i> and from the Land.” Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah, this is Rebbi Ismael’s, since Rebbi Ismael said, the <i>`omer</i> is not brought from Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">And certainly not from impure lands.</i>. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 1:6" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.1.6">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>1:6</a>.</i>: “There are ten levels of holiness. The Land of Israel is holier than other lands; and what is its holiness? That one brings from it the <i>`omer</i>, first fruits, and the Two Breads, which cannot be from other lands.” Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah, this is Rebbi Ismael’s, since Rebbi Ismael said, the <i>`omer</i> is not brought from Syria. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševi`it</i>1:5.</i>: “Rebbi Ismael says, since sowing is a voluntary act, also harvesting is a voluntary act. This excludes harvesting the <i>`omer</i>, which is a commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:21" href="/Exodus.34.21"><i>Ex.</i> 34:21</a>: <i>Six days you may work but on the Seventh Day you must rest, from ploughing and harvesting you must rest</i>. According to R. Ismael while this forbids any optional harvesting on the Sabbath, it implies that harvesting required by a religious commandment must be performed on the Sabbath. (Cf. <i>Ševi`it</i> Chapter 1, Notes 43 and 2.)<br><b>ג</b> adds here the one-sentence <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.1.7">Halakhah <i>Ševi`it</i>1:5 (Note 44</a>).</i>.” Who is the Tanna of “the watchmen for aftergrowth in the sabbatical year take their wages from the disbursement from the lodge”? Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Syria in matters of religious law is the area which was part of David’s empire but not permanently settled by one of the Twelve Tribes. The rules of the Land do not apply biblically but the land is not ritually impure. Therefore grain for use in the Temple could be bought from there.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, it is everybody’s opinion. If one would not find in Syria,.one would bring from the aftergrowth in the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">While not needed every year, in a year of drought in Syria it might be obvious that one has to look for any available grain in the Land.</i>. Could one sow from the start for the `<i>omer</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:3" href="/Leviticus.25.3"><i>Lev.</i> 25:3</a> introducing the Sabbatical year combines sowing and harvesting, one could make the point that only voluntary sowing in a Sabbatical is forbidden, therefore obligatory sowing leading to obligatory harvesting is permitted.</i>? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada asked before Rebbi Mana, would he not take the fistful for leftovers than cannot be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Only a fistful of the grain brought for the <i>`omer</i>(a tenth of an <i>ephah,</i> about 3.8 l) is burned on the altar, the leftover has to be consumed by the priests in the Sanctuary. But produce sown in a Sabbatical is forbidden; the leftover of the flour could not be consumed.</i>? He said to him, it has a status like the five kinds which can be brought in impurity but may not be eaten in impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The leftover has to be burned. The same would happen if all available grain and all available personnel were impure, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.7.4.1">Mishnah <i>Pesaḥim</i>7:4</a>.</i>.
How does he do it<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is that the <i>sheqalim</i> are Temple property which can only be used to buy sacrifices. If the Temple administrator would pay workers with these monies, he would be guilty of <i>me`ilah</i>, larceny of sacred property.</i>? He takes coins from the money changer and gives them to the harvesters and the watchmen before the <i>`omer</i> is presented. He brings coins from the disbursement from the lodge and redeems them for it<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>`omer</i> is presented as flour. Therefore the value of the grain and the cost of all labor used in the preparation of the offering can be added and the <i>sheqalim</i> coins redeemed on the total value of the sacrifice. The redeemed coins can then be used to liquidate the debt incurred at the banker’s.</i>; then it is in order<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of B, <i>Rebbi Tibḥi ben Rebbi Yose</i>, seems to be a grotesque misreading.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abba: Any which he gives are its coins from the start<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The red cow whose ashes purify the impurity of the dead, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>.</i>. It was stated: The same is the case with the stone-masons<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The scapegoat of the day of Atonement.</i>. He takes coins from the money changer and gives them to the quarry workers and the stone-cutters before it is fixed in the row<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The strip of purple wool, together with a branch of cedar and a hyssop, which the Cohen has to throw into the pyre of the burning Cow, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:6" href="/Numbers.19.6"><i>Num</i>. 19:6</a>.</i>. Then he brings coins from the disbursement from the lodge and redeems them for it; then it is in order. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Samuel: Any which he gives are its coins from the start.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The Cow<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The red cow whose ashes purify the impurity of the dead, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>.</i>, the he-goat which is sent away<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The scapegoat of the day of Atonement.</i>, and the shiny strip<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The strip of purple wool, together with a branch of cedar and a hyssop, which the Cohen has to throw into the pyre of the burning Cow, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:6" href="/Numbers.19.6"><i>Num</i>. 19:6</a>.</i> come from the money disbursed from the lodge<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">These are explicitly prescribed by biblical verses.</i>. The ramp for the Cow<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Leading directly from the Temple to the Mount of Olives.</i>, the ramp for the he-goat which is sent away<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The ramp built to shield the scapegoat while it is lead away, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 6:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.6.4.2-5"><i>Yoma</i>6:4</a>.</i>, the strip bound on its horns<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.4.2.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i>4:2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.6.5.1">6:6</a>.</i>, the water canal<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The aqueduct which supplies water to the Temple. The need for a water supply is implicit in the biblical rules for the Tabernacle but is never explicitly mentioned.</i>, the city wall and its towers<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">By biblical rules, family sacrifices may be consumed “in the camp.” The “camp” of the Temple is the walled city of Jerusalem. Therefore the integrity of the walls are a requirement of the Temple service and the necessary expenditures may be made from the Temple tax.</i>, and all needs of the city, come from the leftovers in the lodge<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The items mentioned in this sentence are all necessary or customary parts of the Temple establishment, but are not mentioned in biblical verses. They are paid for from monies not directly used for the service of prior years.</i>. Abba Shaul says, the High Priests build the ramp for the Cow at their own expense.<br>What did one do with the leftover in the lodge<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">What is left from the Temple tax at the end of the fiscal year.</i>? One buys with it wine, oil, and fine flour,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For public sacrifices these are paid for from the coins disbursed from the lodge. Wine, oil, and flour for private sacrifices are bought from the Temple as explained in the next Chapter. R. Ismael holds that the original funds for this operation come from the surplus of the Temple tax; R. Aqiba must hold that this is a self-financing non-profit operation.</i> and the gain is the Temple’s, the words of Rebbi Ismael. Rebbi Aqiba says, one gains neither for the Temple nor funds for the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the Temple may not be involved in commercial transaction. He objects to investing funds destined for the poor not only since avoidance of loss is more important than possible gain but also that money for the poor must be available at all times for possible emergencies.</i>.<br>What did they use the leftover of the disbursement<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">If money was left in the boxes into which it was taken from the lodge at the time when new money was disbursed, the amount of new money was not reduced but the old money was put into a separate account to be used for other needs of the Temple.</i> for? Gold sheets coating for the Holiest of Holies. Rebbi Ismael said, the leftover of produce<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The gain made by the Temple in providing flour, oil, and wine, for private sacrifices.</i> is for adornment of the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">It is given to the gift account to buy elevation sacrifices in times when the altar otherwise would have been idle.</i>, the leftover of the disbursement is for Service vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The vessels used in sacrificial acts.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, the leftover of the disbursement is for adornment of the altar, the leftover of the libations<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:2:7-3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.2.7-3.3">Halakhah 5</a>, the income from the trading arrangements with the suppliers to the Temple.</i> is for Service vessels. Rebbi Ḥanania the executive officer of the Cohanim says, the leftover of the libations is for adornment of the altar, the leftover of the disbursement is for Service vessels. Neither of them did agree about produce<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥanania agrees with R. Aqiba that the Temple has to provide the produce at cost and cannot make a gain on this service.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">From <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 4:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.4.2.2"><i>Yoma</i> Halakhah 4:2</a> (<b>י</b>).</i> Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: There are three strips. The one for the he-goat for a tetradrachma, for the sufferer from skin disease for a sheqel<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For the purification ceremony of the person healed from skin disease (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:4" href="/Leviticus.14.4"><i>Lev.</i> 14:4</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:6" href="/Leviticus.14.6">6</a>).</i>, for the Cow for two tetradrachmas. Rebbi Onias from Berat-Hauran in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Zavda in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Ḥalaphta, for the Cow for two tetradrachmas and a half; but some express it in the formulation ten (<i>zin</i>)<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.5.3"><i>Terumot</i>10:7 (Note 80</a>) as 1/100 of a (probably Roman) pound. 2.5 <i>tetradrachma</i> are 10 <i>denarii</i>. Since an Augustean <i>denar</i> was 1/96 of a Roman pound (silver), 10 <i>zin</i> are 9.6 <i>denarii</i>.<br>The corrector’s reading <i>zuz</i>(denar) is from B.</i> [<i>zuz</i>].
Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If the reading of B were “Rav Huna in the name of Samuel”, it would need consideration as <i>lectio difficilior</i>. But since it is the impossible “Rav Huna in the name of Rebbi Samuel”, it has to be disregarded. <b>ג</b> is defective at this place. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 106a" href="/Ketubot.106a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>106a</a> the statement is attributed to Rav Giddul in the name of Rav.</i>: The scholars who teach the priests the rules of slaughter, rules of receiving, rules of sprinkling, take their wages from the disbursement from the lodge<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since every week another group of Cohanim comes to serve, which may bring with them young inexperienced members of the group, they are permanently occupied. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 106a" href="/Ketubot.106a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>106a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Redifa in the name of Immi: The inspectors of defects of <i>sancta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since the animals chosen for the daily sacrifice have to be without defects (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:3" href="/Numbers.28.3"><i>Num.</i>28:3</a>), there have to be competent daily inspections. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 106a" href="/Ketubot.106a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>106a</a>.</i> take their wages from the disbursement from the lodge. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Simlai: Those who correct the scroll of the Temple courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">From which the High Priest reads on the Day of Atonement and which is used to standardize the texts of copies of the Torah. The reading of B, “Ezra’s scroll”, has to be rejected. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 106a" href="/Ketubot.106a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>106a</a>.</i> take their wages from the disbursement from the lodge. Giddul bar Benjamin in the name of Assi: The two judges of robberies<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This is the translation of the text here and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i>13:1</a>. But it seems that the meaning of the term is that of the reading of <b>ג</b> and most Babylonian sources here and in <i>Ketubot</i>13, דַּיָנֵי גְזֵירוֹת “judges of decisions,” i. e., appeals judges. The reading here is the result of an exchange of liquids. The legal principles attributed to these judges in <i>Ketubot</i> Chapter 13 all refer to matters of civil, mostly matrimonial, law. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 105a" href="/Ketubot.105a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i>105a</a>.</i> take their wages from the disbursement from the lodge. Samuel said, women who are weaving the gobelins<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">For use in the Temple Hall, to separate between the holy and the holiest of holies.</i> take their wages from the disbursement from the lodge; Rav Ḥuna said, from disbursements for repair of the House. How do they disagree? Samuel makes it a sacrifice, Rav Huna makes it part of the building. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said that Rebbi Jehudah Dried Figs<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to determine his time of activity.</i> stated: The incense and all public sacrifices come from the disbursement from the lodge. The golden altar and vessels for the Service come from the excess of libations<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This statement endorses the position of R. Ismael in the Mishnah, that the operation to provide wine, oil, and flour to those who bring private sacrifices, routinely provides a surplus to the Temple.</i>. The altar for elevation sacrifices, the building, and the courtyards<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This statement endorses the position of R. Ismael in the Mishnah, that the operation to provide wine, oil, and flour to those who bring private sacrifices, routinely provides a surplus to the Temple.</i> come from the leftover in the lodge. The outside of the courtyards come from the lodge for maintenance of the House<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The place where the funds are kept which were given as vows to the Temple.</i>. But was it not stated: One commits <i>me`ilah</i> with stones of the Temple or the courtyards<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Me`ilah</i> is defined (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev.</i> 5:15</a>) as “larceny committed on the Eternal’s <i>sancta</i>”, where the usual interpretation is that <i>sancta</i> are only sacrifices and their appurtenances; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 3:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.3.3.4">Halakhah 3:4, Note 66</a>.</i>. How can one commit <i>me`ilah</i> with leftovers? But it must follow Rebbi Meïr, for Rebbi Meïr said, one commits <i>me`ilah</i> with leftovers. Rebbi Ḥinena said, Rebbi Meïr said this only during its year. But here we are after its year<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Money is a <i>sanctum</i> whose misuse is <i>me`ilah</i> only if there is the possibility that it will be used to buy sacrifices. As explained earlier, any leftovers after disbursement still must be kept together since they might be needed if the money disbursed later was not sufficient for the Temple’s needs. But once a new year started, the old <i>sheqalim</i> cannot be used to buy sacrifices of any kind; they cannot cause <i>me`ilah</i>. The <i>baraita</i> is rejected. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 54a" href="/Kiddushin.54a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i>54a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥizqiah said that Rebbi Jehudah Dried Figs<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">It is impossible to determine his time of activity.</i> stated: The table, the candelabrum, the altars<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since obviously in the absence of an altar there can be no sacrifice, the reference is to the golden incense altar in the Sanctuary, not the large altar outside. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 62a" href="/Zevachim.62a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i>62a</a>.</i>, and the gobelins<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">For use in the Temple Hall, to separate between the holy and the holiest of holies.</i> obstruct the sacrifice, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, the sacrifice is only obstructed by the wash basin and its base<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since the priests are forbidden to officiate unless they wash hands and feet from the Temple wash basin on its base, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:17-21" href="/Exodus.30.17-21"><i>Ex.</i> 30:17–21</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 40:30-32" href="/Exodus.40.30-32">40:30–32</a>.</i>. Otherwise, Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina both say, everywhere “opposite<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The following refers to the commandment how the Tabernacle had to be organized (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 26:32-37" href="/Exodus.26.32-37"><i>Ex.</i> 26:32–37</a>; 40:1–16) and the report how this was executed ((<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 40" href="/Exodus.40"><i>Ex.</i> 40:1–17–33</a>.) The candelabrum was put “opposite the table”. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 40:24" href="/Exodus.40.24"><i>Ex.</i> 40:24</a>).</i>” is written it is obstructive, “on the side<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The table has to be put “on the North side”, the candelabrum “on the South side”; (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 26:35" href="/Exodus.26.35"><i>Ex.</i> 26:35</a>).</i>” is not obstructive, but Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan said, even “on the side” is obstructive, and Rebbi Ila in the name of Rebbi Jonathan said, even “putting<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Moses “put” all the furniture of the Tabernacle at its correct places (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 40:18" href="/Exodus.40.18"><i>Ex.</i> 40:18</a> ff.)</i>” is obstructive. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, here for service inside, there for service outside<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since one may bring sacrifices on the altar without a Temple (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 8:6" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.8.6">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i>8:6</a>), for any offering on the large altar one only needs the wash basin and its base. But for any offering inside the Sanctuary, e. g., the daily incense burning, all pieces have to be available at the correct places.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Discussion of the statement of Abba Shaul in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, that the ramp for the Cow (including a bridge over the Kidron valley) was made new every time on the High Priest’s expense. It is implied that the High Priest either oversaw the ceremony himself or appointed one of his sons.</i> Rebbi Ḥanina said, there was much arrogance among the sons of the High Priests, for more than <sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Me`ilah</i> is defined (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev.</i> 5:15</a>) as “larceny committed on the Eternal’s <i>sancta</i>”, where the usual interpretation is that <i>sancta</i> are only sacrifices and their appurtenances; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 3:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.3.3.4">Halakhah 3:4, Note 66</a>.</i> talents of gold<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">That would be 180’000 <i>sheqel</i> or 360’000 <i>denar</i>. One should take note of the reading of B, “silver” instead of “gold”.</i> [did they spend on it.]<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Addition by the corrector, an adaptation to the formulation in B; not supported by any Genizah fragment.</i> If the ramp for the Cow was still standing, no one was leading out his cow on the other’s ramp, but he tore it down and was building it anew. Rebbi Ulla objected before Rebbi Mana: Was it not stated that Simeon the Just made two Cows; on the ramp on which he took out one he did not take out the other<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since he was in office for 40 years, after about 35 years probably not much of the first ramp was still in existence.</i>. Could you say that Simeon the Just was arrogant? What about it? Because they gave eminence to the Cow, ornaments they made for the Cow. It was stated: Ledges and walls were on both sides so that the priests could not look down and become impure<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Kidron valley was used as burial ground at least from First Temple times, if the Cohen was not protected by the wooden floor of the ramp which separated him from the “tent impurity” emanating from the graves, the Cohen would become severely impure and not able to conduct the ceremony. Therefore one made it impossible for the Cohen to lean out over the railings. The scribe’s reading. “could not look down” is preferable over that of the other sources, “leave”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Parah 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Parah.3.5">Tosephta <i>Parah</i>3:7</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This does not belong to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:2:1-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.2.1-6">Mishnah 4</a> but is the discussion of the statement of Rebbi Aqiba that one does not invest capital of funds for the poor.</i> Only if he wants that the loss be his and the gain for the endowment<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">While originally this means “property of the Temple”, it is used in the sense common in the Middle Ages, “endowment of a fund for the poor”, “welfare fund.” The text shows that this includes property administered by a guardian for the benefit of underage orphans.</i> it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">As agreement between adults, that the risk of investments is borne solely by the agent but any gain split between agent and investor, it is forbidden as hidden payment of interest. But if the investment is for a charitable fund for minor orphans it is permitted. Babli <i>Bava Meṣia`</i> 70a.</i>. As the following: Bar Zemina was appointed for property of orphans. He came and asked Rebbi Mana, who told him, if you want that the loss be yours and the gain be split among you it is permitted. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada was appointed for property of orphans and acted in this way.
It<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:2:1-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.2.1-6">Mishnah 4</a> about the leftover of produce, whose existence is denied by R. Aqiba and R. Ḥanina.</i> is all Rebbi Ismael’s. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph explains the Mishnah: The leftover of produce<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The gain made by the Temple in providing flour, oil, and wine, for private sacrifices.</i> is the gain of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The money made by the Temple in selling for a profit flour, oil, and wine, for flour offerings and libations.</i>; the leftover of libations is the fourth <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Mishnah 11, the Temple does not have to hedge its purchases of produce because it is protected against changes in the market place at all times. If the Temple contracted for flour at the rate of 3 <i>seah</i> per tetradrachma and at the time of delivery it was 4 <i>seah</i> per tetradrachma, the provider has to deliver 4. But if the contract was for 4 and the price went up and now stands at 3 for a tetradrachma, the provider has to deliver 4 while the Temple will sell at the going rate.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan explains the Mishnah, The leftover of produce is the fourth <i>seah;</i> the leftover of libations is the overflow<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In order to avoid the sin of <i>me`ilah</i>, the suppliers of produce of all kinds to the Temple have to deliver slightly more than the measure which was contracted for, while the Temple will distribute this product for private libations and flour offerings at the exact measure. The small differences will add up to a considerable amount during a full year; this kind of gain is approved also by the opponents of R. Ismael.</i>. Does Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph not have overflow? Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, what is counted for the fourth <i>seah</i> is overflow<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the reason that the Temple always is the beneficiary of changes in the market place also is to serve as a precaution against <i>me`ilah</i> infractions; both kinds of additions have the same status.</i>. The opinion of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph is understandable. “one does not gain neither for the Temple nor funds for the poor,” therefore “neither of them did agree about produce.” The opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan is difficult. We have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Mishnah 11, the Temple does not have to hedge its purchases of produce because it is protected against changes in the market place at all times. If the Temple contracted for flour at the rate of 3 <i>seah</i> per tetradrachma and at the time of delivery it was 4 <i>seah</i> per tetradrachma, the provider has to deliver 4. But if the contract was for 4 and the price went up and now stands at 3 for a tetradrachma, the provider has to deliver 4 while the Temple will sell at the going rate.</i>: “if the going rate was three, he has to deliver for four” and we have stated, “neither of them did agree about produce.” They did not agree about produce to adorn the altar; they did agree for Service vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since Mishnah 11 is unanimous opinion, the opponents of R. Ismael cannot deny that the Temple always makes money which has to be used for definite purposes.</i>. So far overflow of public {sacrifices}. Even overflow for private {sacrifices}. Would then not Service vessels come from private donations? It is as it was stated, “a woman who made a coat for her son has to surrender it to the public.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This was discussed earlier, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.1.2">Note 14</a>.</i>” So far the overflow of fluids; even the overflow of dry goods; as that which we stated<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 7:4" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.7.4">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i>7:4</a>. This is not directly overflow but a third way in which the Temple accumulates a surplus. If a sacrifice was brought and the offerer bought the libations including the flour offering from the Temple, if then these flour offerings could not be used because the sacrifice was disqualified, the priests in charge may use the flour, etc., for the next sacrifice. In this case the Temple is paid twice for the same produce; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Menachot 10:2" href="/Tosefta_Menachot.10.2">Tosephta <i>Menaḥot</i>10:8</a> states that the money accumulated in this way is given to the gift account to buy elevation offerings for the idle altar.</i>, “in case libations were sanctified in a vessel when the sacrifice was found disqualified, if there is another sacrifice they should be brought with it; otherwise they will become disqualified by staying overnight.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What did one do with the excess of incense<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Which from the start was intended to be sufficient for an intercalary year and therefore had guaranteed leftovers in a regular year.</i>? One sets apart the artisans’ wages and redeems them on the artisan’s money; one gives them to the artisans as their wages and then buys them back from them from the new disbursement<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Since the expenses of the Temple service each year have to be paid by the <i>sheqalim</i> of that year, one cannot simply take the leftover as incense for the next year. On the other hand, since profane use of incense prepared in the proportions prescribed for Temple service is a deadly sin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:38" href="/Exodus.30.38"><i>Ex.</i> 30:38</a>), the incense cannot be used outside of the Temple. One takes money from the <i>sheqalim</i> to pay the artisans who prepare the next year’s batch of incense, then uses this money in order to redeem the incense and make it profane, a possible object of trade. Then one pays the artisans in kind with the incense, and since they could do nothing with it one buys the incense back from them with the money earmarked to this effect. Then the artisans are paid at the same time the incense for the new year is paid with money from the new Temple year starting at Nisan 1.</i>. If the new money is available at its time one takes them from the new disbursement, otherwise from the old<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since the insistence on new money is customary, not biblical, if the new Temple tax was not collected by Nisan 1, one takes the money from the prior disbursement to effect the trade.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.3.1">Mishnah 5</a>. What is gained by redeeming incense with Temple money, which is not profane?</i> Would not Temple property be redeemed by Temple property? What does he do? Rebbi Simeon bar Carsana said, he brings coins and redeems them for the building<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Both the scribe’s text and that of B are difficult to understand. While it is accepted that the Temple is built as profane building and only sanctified when completed (Babli <i>Me`ilah</i>14a), once it is sacred it cannot be redeemed. If the money is legitimately paid for a sacrificial animal, it belongs to the owner of the animal and is no longer available for Temple use. Tosephta <i>Me`ilah</i>1:23 reads: “A worker who did work for the Temple for a <i>mina</i> or two may not say, give me this cow for a <i>mina</i> or this garment for 50 {denar} since Temple property cannot be redeemed for work but only for coins. What does one do? One takes the wages of the artisans {from the <i>sheqalim</i>} and redeems them on the artisans’ money, gives them to the artisans as their wages; then buys them back from the disbursement of the lodge to pay back the workers for the coins they earlier gave to the Temple.” A similar text seems to be intended here.</i>; brings incense and redeems it for them, and gives it<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The incense.</i> to the artisans as their wages. What should be done with those coins? Rebbi says, I am saying that they should be given to the family Garmu and the family Eutinos who were experts in compounding the incense and in making the shew-bread<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 3:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.3.9.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i>3:11</a>.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav &lt;isaac&lt;<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Name added from B.</i> said, only if they were due to them beforehand<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since Temple money can only become profane for actual debts incurred by the Temple. Advance payments are not possible.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba asked, what if they were not due beforehand? There came Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One uses them to adorn the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">It is given to the gift account to buy elevation sacrifices in times when the altar otherwise would have been idle.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cohen asked before Rebbi Yose: The opinion of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba seems inverted. Here it is questionable for him; there it is obvious for him<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">How can he ask when he himself teaches the answer which his teacher had given? He does not question that a possible use is to give the money to the gift account; he questions whether this is the only possible use.<br>The inverse order in B must be a scribal error.</i>. He told him, where it is questionable for him, for Service vessels; where it is obvious for him, to adorn the altar.
<sup class="footnote-marker">100a</sup><i class="footnote">This is a copy from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 5:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.5.1.3"><i>Yoma</i> 5:1, explained there in Notes 12-30</a>. The introductory sentence is missing, but is partially present in B. The question is, why does the Mishnah prescribe that leftover incense should be redeemed and used as payment of the artisans. Since it is a biblical decree (Lev. 27:10) that unblemished animals dedicated as sacrifices cannot be redeemed, would it not be reasonable to assume that sacrificial objects sanctified in a Service vessel cannot be redeemed? If one accepts this, a second question arises. Was the incense prepared in a Service vessel and therefore is sanctified from the start, or does only the daily portion become sanctified when it is filled into a Service vessel, and the leftovers are intrinsically redeemable as Temple property?</i> As they disagreed: If it<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The incense.</i> was compounded as profane, Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, it is disqualified; Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, it is qualified. What is Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina’s reason? <i>It is holy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such verse; similar verses about incense would be <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:36" href="/Exodus.30.36"><i>Ex.</i>30:36</a>, <i>most holy it shall be for you, Ex.</i> 30:37, <i>holy it shall be for you</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 6a" href="/Keritot.6a">Babli <i>Keritut</i> 6a</a>.</i>, that it shall be brought into the Sanctuary. What is Rebbi Joshua ben Levi’s reason? <i>It is holy</i>, that it shall be brought from the disbursement from the lodge. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Bun said, Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina’s parallels Samuel and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi’s Rebbi Joḥanan, as we have stated, “if one dedicated his property to the Temple and there were objects appropriate as public offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.4.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, incense. Rebbi Hoshaia said, explain it about an artisan of the family Eutinos who took incense as his wages. And Rebbi Joshua ben Levi’s is like Samuel, as Rav Ḥuna said in the name of Samuel, they made the mortar a vessel to sanctify. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Ḥuna said this before Rebbi Yose: something sanctified in a vessel may be redeemed. He said to him, is that not Samuel’s? Since Samuel said, one is lenient in the case of leftovers. As they disagreed: If unblemished animals were left over, Samuel says, they are redeemed unblemished. Rebbi Joḥanan said, they are redeemed as disqualified <i>sancta</i>. Leftover he-goats, in Samuel’s opinion if elevation sacrifices are redeemed, a purification sacrifice so much more. In Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion? Rebbi Ze`ira said, they shall graze. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, one uses them to adorn the altar. This is difficult. May a purification sacrifice be brought as elevation sacrifice? Rebbi Yose said, there is a difference, for public sacrifices are determined only by slaughter. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, it is a stipulation of the Court that all leftovers should be brought as elevation sacrifices.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody gives his property to the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple is supposed to sell the property and to use the proceeds for its needs. As Temple property, the proceeds are under the laws of <i>me`ilah</i>.</i> and in it there were items usable for public sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Which cannot be redeemed, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.3.3">Note 100</a>.</i>, they shall be given to the artisans as their wages, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. Ben Azzai said to him, this is not they way<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Since Temple property cannot be given for wages, Tosephta <i>Me`ilah</i>1:23, Note 95. One has to apply the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.3.1">Mishnah 5</a> in their entirety.</i>, but from it one sets apart the artisans’ wages and redeems it on the artisan’s money; one gives it to the artisans as their wages and then buys it back from them from the new disbursement<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Since the expenses of the Temple service each year have to be paid by the <i>sheqalim</i> of that year, one cannot simply take the leftover as incense for the next year. On the other hand, since profane use of incense prepared in the proportions prescribed for Temple service is a deadly sin (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:38" href="/Exodus.30.38"><i>Ex.</i> 30:38</a>), the incense cannot be used outside of the Temple. One takes money from the <i>sheqalim</i> to pay the artisans who prepare the next year’s batch of incense, then uses this money in order to redeem the incense and make it profane, a possible object of trade. Then one pays the artisans in kind with the incense, and since they could do nothing with it one buys the incense back from them with the money earmarked to this effect. Then the artisans are paid at the same time the incense for the new year is paid with money from the new Temple year starting at Nisan 1.</i>.<br>If somebody gives his property to the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple is supposed to sell the property and to use the proceeds for its needs. As Temple property, the proceeds are under the laws of <i>me`ilah</i>.</i> and in it there was an animal fit for the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">An unblemished bovine, sheep, or goat, which may not be redeemed, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev.</i> 27:10</a>. Since they were given to the Temple to be sold, the easiest way to satisfy the will of the donor and the rules is to sell the animals as sacrifices to those who have vowed a sacrifice.</i>, males or females. Rebbi Eliezer says, males should be sold to those who need an elevation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Who in contrast to well-being offerings must be male (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:3" href="/Leviticus.1.3"><i>Lev.</i> 1:3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:10" href="/Leviticus.1.10">10</a>).</i>, and females be sold to those who need well-being offerings, and the revenue together with the remainder be for upkeep of the Temple.<br>Rebbi Joshua says, the males themselves should be brought as elevation sacrifices, and the females be sold to those who need well-being offerings, and the remaining property be used for upkeep of the Temple.<br>Rebbi Aqiba says, I agree with the words of Rebbi Eliezer against the words of Rebbi Joshua, since Rebbi Eliezer is uniform in his rules but Rebbi Joshua splits<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Eliezer the entire property is for the upkeep of the Temple; R. Joshua excludes male unblemished animals. Also R. Eliezer treats male and female animals by the same rules.</i>. Rebbi Pappaeus said, I heard according to the words of both of them. If somebody dedicates explicitly<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">He says, the animals are to be sacrifices.</i>, following the words of Rebbi Eliezer; if the dedication is unspecified<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">He simply hands over his property to the Temple.</i>, following the words of Rebbi Joshua.<br>If somebody gives his property to the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple is supposed to sell the property and to use the proceeds for its needs. As Temple property, the proceeds are under the laws of <i>me`ilah</i>.</i> and in it there were things fit for the altar, wines, oils, or birds. Rebbi Eleazar says, they shall be sold to people needing these kinds and from the proceeds one shall bring elevation sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Four-legged animals. Even though birds (pigeons and turtle doves) are valid elevation sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:14-17" href="/Leviticus.1.14-17"><i>Lev.</i> 1:14–17</a>), only four-legged animals can be brought “to adorn the altar.”</i>; but the remainder of the property shall be used for the upkeep of the Temple.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody gives his property to the Temple and in it there were items usable for any public sacrifices.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, incense. Rebbi Hoshaia said, explain it about an artisan of the family Eutinos who took incense as his wages. What is Ben Azzai’s reason? For Temple property cannot be redeemed by work, only by coins.
There we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:2" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.2">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i>7:2</a>.</i>: “There is about gifts for the upkeep of the Temple that unspecified gifts to the Temple are for the upkeep of the Temple. Sanctification for the upkeep of the Temple falls on everything<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Anything of value can be donated to the Temple.</i>, and <i>me`ilah</i> applies to what grows from it<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Not only growth from seeds donated but all income from the principal are protected by <i>me`ilah</i> laws as is the original donation.</i>, and priests have no usufruct from it.” Rebbi Ḥanania said, this is Rebbi Eliezer’s, as we have stated: “If somebody gives his property to the Temple and in it there was an animal fit for the altar, males or females. Rebbi Eliezer says, males should be sold to those who need an elevation offering, and females be sold to those who need well-being offerings, and the revenue together with the remainder be for upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah starts “if somebody gives his property,” this means it is unspecified, given to the Temple to use it as it sees fit, and R. Eliezer says that all proceeds go to the upkeep of the Temple.</i>.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of Rebbi Eliezer : <i>If a man dedicate his house holy to the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:14" href="/Leviticus.27.14"><i>Lev.</i> 27:14</a>.</i>. How do we hold? If for a dwelling, it already is written, <i>if the dedicator redeem his house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:15" href="/Leviticus.27.15"><i>Lev.</i> 27:15</a>.</i>. Therefore we hold that he dedicates his property. From here that unspecified dedications to the Temple are for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:14" href="/Leviticus.27.14">Verse 14</a> states that if a person dedicates his house, a priest has to determine its value. V. 15 the notes that if the donor wants to regain his house, he has to pay 125% of the value. Since it is assumed that a dwelling is always needed, the donation of a house could have been described in one sentence, combining valuation and redemption. Since the verses are split, it is understood that also property not used as dwelling is included and is given to produce money for the Temple, not the priests (in contrast to agricultural property, vv.21.</i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira, Rav Huna in the name of Rav: They disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.4.1">Mishnah 7</a> and R. Joshua in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.4.1">Mishnah 8</a>.</i> about one who gives his property to the Temple. But if he gives his flock to the Temple, everybody agrees that it is for the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The animals should not be sold for the benefit of the Temple to people who need sacrificial animals but be directly used as elevation offerings of the Temple in times of need. The only animals to be sold are the blemished ones.</i>. Rebbi Abba, Rav Huna in the name of Rav: Where do they disagree? About one who gives his flock to the Temple, but if he gave his property to the Temple, everybody agrees that it is for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding paragraph.</i>. The opinion of Rebbi Ze`ira can be understood<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since an animal becomes a sacrifice only by a dedication, the animals given to the Temple in a will, while becoming subject to the laws of <i>me`ilah</i> as Temple property, are not sacrifices and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9-10" href="/Leviticus.27.9-10"><i>Lev.</i>27:9–10</a> does not apply to them. The disagreement between RR. Eliezer and Joshua is purely about rabbinic rules and these apply equally to both cases.</i>. It is difficult for Rebbi Abba. Is an animal not for the altar? An animal is for the altar. But why did this man not specify? It is as if he said, it only should be for the upkeep of the Temple. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no difference. The disagreement is both about one who gives his property to the Temple as about one who gives his flock to the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since an animal becomes a sacrifice only by a dedication, the animals given to the Temple in a will, while becoming subject to the laws of <i>me`ilah</i> as Temple property, are not sacrifices and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9-10" href="/Leviticus.27.9-10"><i>Lev.</i>27:9–10</a> does not apply to them. The disagreement between RR. Eliezer and Joshua is purely about rabbinic rules and these apply equally to both cases.</i>.
Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: <i>Sancta</i> given for the upkeep of the Temple which were redeemed unblemished become profane<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">While dedicated unblemished animals cannot be redeemed and cannot revert to profane status, animals willed to the Temple are not in this category as explained in the preceding Note. While selling the animals to be used as sacrifice is the prescribed proceeding, if the rules are not followed and they are sold for profane use the sale is valid on condition that the Temple receive its money.</i>. A Mishnah says so<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 10:2" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.10.2">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i>10:2</a>. The Mishnah states that blemished animals which were dedicated as sacrifices never become intrinsically holy; if they are redeemed they are fully profane, their offspring and their milk is profane (first quote). If they were validly dedicated and later developed a blemish, they have to be redeemed and used as profane food, but cannot totally lose their sacred status; they cannot be used for work nor are their offspring and their milk permitted (second quote, also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.1"><i>Temurah</i>7:1</a>.)</i>, “their offspring and their milk are permitted after their redemption.” Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rav Ḥisda: Explain it if they were redeemed unblemished and then became defective. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rav Ḥisda: Does the Mishnah say so<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 10:2" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.10.2">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i>10:2</a>. The Mishnah states that blemished animals which were dedicated as sacrifices never become intrinsically holy; if they are redeemed they are fully profane, their offspring and their milk is profane (first quote). If they were validly dedicated and later developed a blemish, they have to be redeemed and used as profane food, but cannot totally lose their sacred status; they cannot be used for work nor are their offspring and their milk permitted (second quote, also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.1"><i>Temurah</i>7:1</a>.)</i>? “Their offspring and their milk are forbidden after their redemption.” Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Yose: <i>Sancta</i> given for the upkeep of the Temple which were redeemed unblemished become profane. If you are saying they did not become profane, how could the sanctity of the altar fall on <i>sancta</i> given for the upkeep of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since what is dedicated for one category of <i>sancta</i> cannot be changed to another, both R. Eliezer and R. Joshua must agree that the sale of the animal by the Temple makes it profane; the dedication of the buyer is the valid dedication of a profane animal.</i>?
The sanctity of the altar falls on defective animals. In which respect? For shearing and work<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">It is sinful to dedicate a defective animal to the altar. The question is whether such a dedication is effective at all. The statement here shows that the animal has to be treated according to the rules of an animal dedicated unblemished which later developed a defect (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.4.6">Note 121</a>). B correctly adds prohibition of its offspring or its milk.</i>. “<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 9:19" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).9.19">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i>9:19</a>, <i>Temurah</i>2:5; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 19b-20a" href="/Temurah.19b-20a">Babli <i>Temurah</i>19b/20a</a>. The text of B in the anonymous statement, “does not effect substitution”, is a scribal error as shown by the later text in B.<br>The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev.</i> 27:10</a>, that it is forbidden to make a substitution for a dedicated animal, but if such a substitution was made, both the original animal and its substitute are <i>sancta</i>. Therefore “to effect substitution” implies “the original dedication is valid.” Everybody agrees that even if the dedication is void as dedication for the altar, it is valid as a gift of the animal or its value for the upkeep of the Temple.</i> If somebody dedicated a female for his elevation offering, or his <i>Pesaḥ</i>, or his reparation offering, it effects substitution. Rebbi Simeon says, for his elevation offering it effects substitution; for his <i>Pesaḥ</i> or his reparation offering it does not effect substitution.” “<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">This is known only as a quote in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 20b" href="/Temurah.20b">Babli, <i>Temurah</i>20b</a>.</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Simeon, neither for his elevation offering, nor his <i>Pesaḥ</i>, nor his reparation offering, does it effect substitution.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of Rebbi Simeon is that we find that a female is qualified as elevation offering of a bird<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Male animals are prescribed for four-legged elevation sacrifices, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:3" href="/Leviticus.1.3"><i>Lev.</i> 1:3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:10" href="/Leviticus.1.10">10</a>, but not for birds, 1:14 (<i>Sifra Wayyiqra I Parshata</i>6(2,5). Argument missing in B.</i>. [And]<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, unwarranted.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, the reason of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">While dedicated unblemished animals cannot be redeemed and cannot revert to profane status, animals willed to the Temple are not in this category as explained in the preceding Note. While selling the animals to be used as sacrifice is the prescribed proceeding, if the rules are not followed and they are sold for profane use the sale is valid on condition that the Temple receive its money.</i> is, if there are differences in its own kind, so much more if it is not its own kind. What are differences in its own kind? As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 10:2" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.10.2">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i>10:2</a>. The Mishnah states that blemished animals which were dedicated as sacrifices never become intrinsically holy; if they are redeemed they are fully profane, their offspring and their milk is profane (first quote). If they were validly dedicated and later developed a blemish, they have to be redeemed and used as profane food, but cannot totally lose their sacred status; they cannot be used for work nor are their offspring and their milk permitted (second quote, also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:1" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.1"><i>Temurah</i>7:1</a>.)</i>, “a yearling as reparation offering, and he brought one of two years, he satisfied his obligation; a two-year old as reparation offering, and he brought one of three years, he did not satisfy his obligation”. Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Simeon and Rebbi Joshua said the same. As Rebbi Joshua said, a female as elevation offering is sanctified only for its money’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">If willing one’s property to the Temple were a genuine dedication, the animal which was unblemished at the time of donation but later developed a defect never could regain full profane status. Therefore the rule only is possible if the position of R. Joḥanan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.4.4">Note 119</a>) is adopted.</i>, so Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since what is dedicated for one category of <i>sancta</i> cannot be changed to another, both R. Eliezer and R. Joshua must agree that the sale of the animal by the Temple makes it profane; the dedication of the buyer is the valid dedication of a profane animal.</i> said, a female as elevation offering is sanctified only for its money’s worth. If you would say that they are <i>sancta</i> as to their body, they should graze<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence added to the truncated text of B repeats the prior argument. Animals which are leftovers are animals bought with <i>sheqel</i> money but not used at the end of the tax year. Since these animals become dedicated only at the moment they are used (end of the text for <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.3.3">Note 100</a>), their status as far as dedication goes is identical to that of animals willed to the Temple.</i>. “Rebbi said, I agree with Rebbi Simeon about the <i>Pesah</i>; could the <i>Pesaḥ</i> be brought as a well being offering? But I do not agree with the words of Rebbi Simeon for reparation offerings; could a reparation offering be brought as an elevation offering?<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">It is sinful to dedicate a defective animal to the altar. The question is whether such a dedication is effective at all. The statement here shows that the animal has to be treated according to the rules of an animal dedicated unblemished which later developed a defect (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.4.6">Note 121</a>). B correctly adds prohibition of its offspring or its milk.</i>” Rebbi Abbin said, in case a <i>Pesaḥ</i> be brought as a well being offering, its body is brought as well-being offering; in case a reparation offering be brought as an elevation offering, its body cannot be brought as elevation offering. What about it? In one case one says, it is sanctified for its money’s worth; in one case one says, it is sanctified in its body<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Pesachim (Lieberman) 9:19" href="/Tosefta_Pesachim_(Lieberman).9.19">Tosephta <i>Pesaḥim</i>9:19</a>, <i>Temurah</i>2:5; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 19b-20a" href="/Temurah.19b-20a">Babli <i>Temurah</i>19b/20a</a>. The text of B in the anonymous statement, “does not effect substitution”, is a scribal error as shown by the later text in B.<br>The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:10" href="/Leviticus.27.10"><i>Lev.</i> 27:10</a>, that it is forbidden to make a substitution for a dedicated animal, but if such a substitution was made, both the original animal and its substitute are <i>sancta</i>. Therefore “to effect substitution” implies “the original dedication is valid.” Everybody agrees that even if the dedication is void as dedication for the altar, it is valid as a gift of the animal or its value for the upkeep of the Temple.</i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. The reason of Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Joshua requires that the male animals be used as elevation offerings, why does he not require that the females be used directly as well-being offerings, but sold and the proceeds used for elevation offerings?</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:18-19" href="/Leviticus.22.18-19"><i>Lev.</i> 22:18–19</a>.</i><i>Speak to Aaron, and his sons, and to all Children of Israel, and say to them: each single man from Israel</i>, etc. <i>who will</i><i>offer an elevation sacrifice to the Eternal</i>, anything could be elevation sacrifice, <i>voluntarily from you, unblemished, male.</i> From where even females? The verse says, <i>in cattle</i>, to include females<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:3" href="/Leviticus.1.3"><i>Lev.</i> 1:3</a>, whereמִן הַבָּקָר  clearly is partitive, only the select from the cattle, here בַּבָּקָר, “in the herd”, is inclusive. While females cannot be elevation sacrifices, they can be dedicated that their proceeds be used for elevation sacrifices.</i>. Rebbi Isaac ben Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">B adds, probably correctly, “asked”.</i>: It says <i>male</i>, and you are saying <i>in cattle</i>, to include females. Then similarly it is written <i>unblemished</i>, and you are saying <i>in cattle</i>, to include the blemished<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Since Mishnah 7 starts “if there was an animal fit for the altar,” clearly excluding defective animals also according to R. Joshua, the argument is invalid and so is the prior statement of R. Ze`ira.</i>?
What is between them? The stick of a weaver’s shuttle is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">There is very little between them since R. Aqiba only says that R. Eliezer is more logically consistent; he does not decide that practice has to follow R. Eliezer. The weaver’s shuttle is Greek κερκίς, -ίδος. The reading of B, “the statement of Bar Pada” does not seem to make any sense; probably it is a misreading of the version of M, “a matter of invention”, i. e., of formulation.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. The reason of Rebbi Eleazar: <sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:18-19" href="/Leviticus.22.18-19"><i>Lev.</i> 22:18–19</a>.</i><i>Speak to Aaron and to his sons</i>, etc., <i>who would sacrifice to the Eternal as elevation sacrifice;</i> everything may be brought as elevation sacrifice, <i>by your volition, unblemished, male</i>. I could think that this includes birds. The verse says, <i>in cattle</i>, not birds<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse restricts elevation offerings from four-legged animals to unblemished males, it is inferred that the restriction does not apply to birds [<i>Sifra Emor Parashah</i>7(20), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:1-5:26" href="/Leviticus.1.1-5.26"><i>Wayyiqra I Parshata</i>6(3</a>)]. In addition, since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:14-17" href="/Leviticus.1.14-17"><i>Lev.</i> 1:14–17</a> is addressed to the individual, but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:18-19" href="/Leviticus.22.18-19"><i>Lev.</i> 22:18–19</a> to the public, it is inferred that birds as elevation offerings are possible only to the individual; the public is restricted to four-legged animals. Since a gift to the Temple is a gift to the public, birds given to the Temple as part of an estate may not be sacrificed.</i>.
